COLOMBIA
POWER DEVELOPMENT FINANCE PROJECT
FINANCIERA ELECTRICA NACIONAL
SUMMARY
The project aims at assisting Colombia during 1984-85 to maintain
the construction rhythm of ongoing electricity development projects, which
will supply the electric energy needed to facilitate growth of industrial and
commercial activities, as well as help supply electricity to a larger segment
of the country's population, only 54% of which now have access to it. The
participation of the international capital market, which had diminished
sharply from past years, has been enlisted in this.l/ The proposed project
also seeks to support the development of FEN, the recently-established finan-
cial arm of the power sector, created to tap domestic and external capital
markets on its behalf. Support is provided under the project to assist FEN
in its role of strengthening power sector financial management. The proposed
project consists of subprojects representing a two-year time-slice of prior-
ity ongoing electricity development investments, several of which are already
partly financed with Bank loans. Criteria and procedures for subproject
selection have been agreed with FEN. Based upon these criteria, a group of
subprojects has been selected on a preliminary basis, with final selection
and sub-loan amounts to be based upon detailed proposals in each case. Sub-
projects will consist of a portion of the civil works, goods and services
associated with the investments, including interest during construction on
existing Bank loans where justified, and the proposed subloans to be made by
FEN to the utilities would finance foreign exchange costs not covered from
other sources. Procurement under the proposed Bank loan, as well as the
Bank's share of the cofinancing loans, would be under international competi-
tive bidding in line with Bank guidelines. The project would be implemented
over a 2-year period, beginning in the second quarter of 1984.
1/ Approval of the Board of Executive Directors of the negotiated terms and
conditions of proposed US$30 million Bank participation in the US$200
million B-loans is to be sought on the basis of President's Memoranda
distributed separately.






1. THE POWER SECTOR
Energy Resources and Policies
1.01      Colombia is rich in energy resources, particularly hydroelectricity
and coal. Its reserves of oil and natural gas are modest by international
standards, yet significant at the national level. In terms of known
reserves, about 55% of Colombia's primary energy potential lies in hydroelec-
tricity, 40% in coal and only 5% in oil and natural gas. In contrast, con-
sumption of commercial energy relies primarily upon oil products (50%) and
less on natural gas (20%), coal (22%) and hydroelectricity (8%).
1.02      Colombia traditionally enjoyed a positive trade balance in energy
because of its relatively rich energy endowment and modest consumption (at
about one ton of coal equivalent per annum, Colombia's per capita energy con-
sumption is below average for middle-income developing countries). In 1976
the country became a net importer of energy, and by 1981 oil imports reached
almost US$400 million. This came about because of a decline in total domes-
tic energy production, resulting from a 7% annual fall in oil output between
1970 and 1979. As this situation was threatening to compromise balance of
payments prospects, the Government moved in the late-1970s to redress the
negative energy trade balance, through: (i) pricing measures directed at
increasing the overall cost of energy to consumers, at changing relative
prices to encourage consumers to move away from oil, and providing incentives
to producers; and (ii) direct public sector investments in the energy field,
particularly hydroelectricity and coal. As a result, the previous trend has
been reversed since 1980, with energy production increases in petroleum,
aggregate energy consumption growth lower than GDP, and petroleum's total
primary energy consumption falling, while that of hydroelectricity, coal and
natural gas increased.
1.03      Known oil reserves at the end of 1981 totalled about 700 million
barrels, or only about 10 years consumption at present rates of utilization.
Policies pursued during the 1960s and the early 1970s contributed to a dete-
riorating oil situation. Prices to consumers and producers were kept artifi-
cially low, thus promoting consumption and dampening incentives for develop-
ment of new reserves through exploration. In recent years the Government
moved forcefully to remedy this by bringing internal consumer prices closer
to international levels (internal prices were increased by more than three-
fold in real terms between 1974 and 1980, and, lately, have fluctuated around
80-90% of international prices) and by enabling producers to benefit from a
large part of the price increases. As a result, exploration has increased
from 11 wells per year in 1975 to 61 in 1981, with known reserves expanding
by about 10% in 1981, after several years of steady decline. Also, crude oil
production has been increasing at over 5% per annum since 1980. Crude oil
production stood at almost 150,000 bpd at the end of 1982, covering about 80%
of domestic consumption. Since average growth in consumption has not sur-
passed 0.5% per annum during the last five years, self-sufficiency in oil may
be within reach by the mid-1980s. Over the longer term, however, a re-
emergence of oil imports appears likely, as geological prospects for dis-
coveries have been not too encouraging so far.



-2-
1.04      Measured natural gas reserves stood at about 4.2 trillion cubic
feet at the end of 1981, or almost 40 years consumption at present rates of
utilization.  Most reserves, production and consumption are concentrated on
the northern coast, with natural gas being piped to the main population and
industrial centers along the coast.  Growth in gas consumption (which aver-
aged about 7% per annum over the last five years) has been provided a major
push by conversion of some power plants from fuel oil to gas. Thermal power
plants now account for about half of total gas consumption. Industry repre-
sents the bulk of the remainder with oil refineries also important consu-
mers. At present there is significant excess supply in the northern region.
Further important increases in gas consumption are tied largely to several
new gas-processing options currently being studied. The best alternative
appears to be an ammonia-urea plant (the Bank is the Executing Agency for a
UNDP-financed feasibility study).
1.05      As a consequence of the natural gas surplus on the northern coast,
the Government has not yet defined a clear pricing policy for natural gas.
Prices for new deliveries are negotiated ex-post with producers on a case by
case basis. This acts as a disincentive for exploration. Although consumer
prices are currently relatively low (less than US$2 per thousand cubic feet),
the extent to which they result in misallocation of resources is not clear
because the viability of future gas-consuming projects remains to be estab-
lished.
1.06      Colombia's coal resources are substantial, with reserves estimated
at some 16 billion tons, of which only about 20% can be classified as
measured. At present rates of utilization, reserves would last hundreds of
yearsz In view of the magnitude of reserves, Government policy is to encour-
age domestic consumption in substitute for natural gas and fuel oil, and to
promote coal exports, mainly from El Cerrejon but also from other deposits
located near the Atlantic Coast. Since 1979, the UNDP and the Bank have been
working jointly with the State coal corporation, CARBOCOL, that together with
an EXXON subsidiary is developing a 15-mtpy production from El Cerrejon
North. A proposed Bank-financed Coal Exploration Project will assist in
assessing the economic potential of several other promising areas in the
Atlantic and Central Regions.
1.07      Coal production grew at about 7% per annum on average over the
1970s to reach a level of about 5 million tons in 1981. Production came from
about 400 small and medium-scale mines, virtually all of which are non-
mechanized, run by the private sector and located in Central Colombia. More
than half the production and consumption of coal is concentrated in the high-
lands near Bogota. Sixty percent of all coal is consumed by industry, and
most of the rest by the power sector. Residential consumption and exports
represent only a small share of total output. Colombian coal is bituminous,
of high calorific value (mostly over 6,000 kcal/kg) and with low sulphur con-
tent (mostly less than 1%). Some coal in central Colombia possesses coking
properties. In 1982, domestic coal prices, which are freely set by market
forces, typically ranged between US$25-35 per ton delivered to large consu-
mers in the industrial centers of Bogota, Medellin and Cali. This is well
below international prices, but high internal transport costs imposed by the
long distance to ports and difficult terrain all but rule out for now coal
exports from the interior of the country.



-3-
1.08      Colombia's hydroelectric potential, at about 100 GW, is amongst the
largest in the world. Although the country has made strides in developing
this potential (hydroelectric power generation grew at about 10% per annum
during the 1970s), total installed hydro-capacity by end-1982 was less than
4 GW. However, plants now under construction will virtually double capacity
by 1989. Hydroelectric plants currently represent about 70% of total
electric power generation and thermal plants the rest. The largest part of
the thermal capacity is located on the northern coast, where hydro potential
is limited, but where there is abundant coal and natural gas. Since the
plentiful hydro-reserves can be developed at relatively low cost, they
represent an option of high priority. However, the optimal mix of hydro and
coal and gas-fired plants needs to be determined in order to take advantage
of the projected increased supply of coal and the existing availability of a
natural gas surplus. This matter is currently being studied (para. 1.20).
Investment and Financial Issues
1.09      In Colombia, electricity, petroleum and natural gas prices are set
by the Government; coal prices are market-set. In spite of positive Govern-
ment actions in recent years, there is still a need to develop long-term
pricing policies that reflect the opportunity cost of the various energy
resources and provide, within the Government's broader objectives, appro-
priately balanced incentives for the development of energy resources.   It is
not clear whether current prices actually provide such incentives. The
absence of adequate knowledge about some of the investment requirements of
different energy sources, particularly coal, has made it difficult to set out
an integrated investment program and adequate incentives for each subsector.
Towards this end, the Government has undertaken a major effort to improve
sector knowledge through a National Energy Study (ENE), the first stage of
which was carried out between 1979 and 1982 by the National Planning Depart-
ment (DNP) with the help of local consultants and technical assistance from
UNDP and the Federal Republic of Germany (FRG). The report on ENE's first
stage is under Government review at present. This study provides significant
information needed for energy planning, including preliminary evaluation of
energy demand growth and investments that could best serve such growth. How-
ever, much work remains to be done, particularly in the selection of alterna-
tive energy sources. A second stage of the ENE is now commencing with
several specialized studies.l/ The current effort has already elicited
foreign technical assistance from official sources. The FRG will be provid-
ing assistance on modeling and data gathering, France on industrial energy
savings, Italy on rural electrification, and the Organization of American
States (OAS) on energy use in transportation. Several energy sector agencies
and the Ministry of Public Works will provide counterpart staff and DNP will
coordinate the external assistance. Finally, in support of Government
efforts to sharpen its energy investment strategy, the Bank is preparing to
carry out a study on selective issues through the energy assessment program
financed by UNDP.
1.10      Over the past five years, Colombia has been successful in mobiliz-
ing external resources to finance energy development. This has been achieved
/  Among others, studies on natural gas available for ammonia-urea fertili-
zer production and the aforementioned coal exploration studies.



-4-
through direct foreign investment (in oil and gas first and lately in coal)
and external loans to the energy agencies, including power companies. How-
ever, the current conditions in the international lending markets are making
external financing more difficult to obtain. Local financing requirements
are covered by internally generated resources and Government contributions,
with the incipient local capital market providing to date only a marginal
share of financing.  In order to increase internal resource mobilization by
the main energy sector agencies, prices charged to consumers of petroleum
products and electricity have been significantly increased in real terms.
Furthermore, in 1980 the Government created the National Coal Fund (FNC) that
receives revenue from a tax on coal production valued at the mine-head, which
is currently allocated to finance coal exploration (80%) and to assist small
and medium-scale coal mining operations (20%). In 1982 a power development
bank--the borrower for the proposed project--was established to mobilize
domestic as well as foreign credit for the power sector.
Institutional Arrangements in the Power Sector
1.11      Through the 1950s, a large number of power companies were set up by
local governmental authorities and, lacking interconnection with each other,
were operated independently in response to regional and departmental needs.
Some 14 years ago, the country's total electricity ca acity stood at a mere
1,681 MW (equivalent to about 88 watts per inhabitant) and reached only about
'35% of the population. Although large hydropower sites held the potential of
iincreasing substantially the supply of electricity at lower cost than other-
wise possible, they were too big to be tapped in any one company. With en-
couragement from the Bank, the Government persuaded the regional power compa-
nies to break with tradition and pool their resources in a shared effort to
develop the country's large hydroelectric potential. To accomplish this,
Interconexion Electrica, S.A. (ISA) was created as an independent, national
generation and interconnection company, of which the shareholders now include
atll the largest municipal power util ties and the Government-owned power com-
panies. In addition to plant construction, ISA has integrated the regional
power systems. At the same time, the Government has taken measures to
improve the sector's prospects for efficient growth; it established the
National Tariff Board (JNT) in DNP to approve requests from the power compa-
nies for tariff increases, and it fostered the consolidation of numerous
small utilities, particularly in the North Atlantic Region. The Bank parti-
cipated actively in the creation of both ISA and JNT.
1.12      At present, public electricity service is provided by:
(a) municipally-owned companies, independent of the National Govern-
ment, of which the largest are Empresa de Energia Electrica de
Bogota (EEEB), Empresas Publicas de Medellin (EPM), and Empresas
Municipales de Cali (EMCALI);
(b) national enterprises such as Instituto Colombiano de Energia
Electrica (ICEL), and Corporacion Autonoma Regional del Cauca
(CVC), and the Corporacion Electrica de la Costa Atlantica
(CORELCA). ICEL and CORELCA have a total of 21 electrificadoras,
subsidiaries which mainly distribute power at the local level; and



-5-
(d) a generating and transmission company, ISA, the main shareholders
of which are EEEB, EPM, CVC, ICEL and CORELCA.
The Ministry of Mines and Energy owns part of the sector directly through
ICEL and CORELCA. EEEB and EPM, currently the largest utilities, are con-
trolled by autonomous municipal governments. CVC reports to DNP. A brief
description of each principal utility follows below.
1.13      ISA.  Established in 1967 as a public corporation, ISA has provided
a cohesive framework for sector development by creating a national grid
capable of transmitting large amounts of power and energy among the
shareholders who, in turn distribute the energy to consumers in the region of
Colombia. By ISA's undertaking development of large hydroelectric resources
on behalf of its shareholders, they have been able to share the costs and
benefits of the country'slow-cost hydropower potential, which is
geographically concentrated in the areas of Bogota (EEEB) and Medellin
(EPM). The holdings of ISA's shareholders as of December 31, 1983, are:
Utility                     Shares                  _
EEEB                        15,762                25.0
EPM                         15,762                25.0

ICEL                         9,219                14.6
CHEC b/                        239                 0.4
CORELCA                      9,458                15.0
63,050               100.0
a/   A CVC subsidiary.
b/   An ICEL subsidiary.
Headquartered in Medellin, ISA is administered by its Shareholder Assembly, a
5-member Board of Directors and a General Manager supported by a competent
technical staff. The Assembly must act on all major issues and, to ensure
that decisions are based on a consensus, ISA's Bylaws specify that the Share-
holders Assembly achieve the concurrence of 75% of the shares. Decision-
making tends to be slow when the interests of the various shareholders
differ. ISA has constructed 500 MW Chivor I (681-C0, 1972) and 500 MW
Chivor II hydro plants, as well as 66 MW of thermal capacity at Zipaquira and
133 M4W of gas turbine capacity at Chinu. It also has under construction the
1,240 MW San Carlos I and II hydro plants and the 18 MW Calderos hydro
plant (1582-C0, 1978, and 1725-CO, 1981) for 1984/85 operation, the Jaguas
hydro plant (170 MW) for 1984 and the fifth unit at the Zipaquira thermal
plant (66 MW) for 1985.
1.14      EEEB.  An autonomous company, EEEB is owned by the Municipality of
the Special District of Bogota, which generates, transmits and distributes
electricity. It serves some 622,000 consumers in metropolitan Bogota direct-
ly and sells power in bulk to a local distributor in the department of
Cundinamarca. EEEB is administered by a 7-member Board of Directors and a
General Manager. The Mayor of Bogota, or his representative, serves as the
Chairman of the Board of Directors. The General Manager, who is responsible



-6-
for the day-to-day management of the company, is appointed by the Board sub-
ject to the Mayor's approval. EEEB's management is adequate for the execu-
tion of its expansion program and for the operation of its facilities.  EEEB
has an installed capacity of 711 MW (581 MW hydro and 130 MW thermal).   The
company has under construction the 600 MW Mesitas (1628-CO, 1979), now
nearing completion, and the 1,000 MW Guavio hydro projects (2008-CO, 1982),
as well as a Bogota Distribution project (1807-CO, 1981).
1.15      EPM.  An autonomous company, EPM is owned by the Municipality of
Medellin, which provides power, water, sewerage, and telephone services for
the city of Medellin and vicinity. It serves some 350,000 power consumers in
metropolitan Medellin and sells in bulk to a distributing company in the
department of Antioquia. EPM, a well-managed company, is headed by a
7-member Board of Directors and a General Manager. The Mayor of Medellin, or
his representative, is the Chairman of the Board of Directors. The various
services of EPM are managed as separate entities with separate accounting
systems. EPM has an installed capacity of 1,002 MW which will increase to
1,215 MW with the completion of the Guadalupe IV project in 1985. The Bank
is providing financing for Guadalupe IV (1868-CO, 1981) and the 200 MW Playas
hydro project (1953-CO, 1982), the latter expected to be in operation in
1987.
1.16      CVC.  An autonomous multipurpose State corporation under DNP, CVC
is responsible for developing the Cauca Valley and has been discharging its
mandate with notable success. One of its main activities is generating and
transmitting power in the Valley, undertaken in part through CHIDRAL, which
is owned by CVC (65%), the Municipality of Cali (17%) and EMCALI (18%).
CVC-CHIDRAL sells power in bulk to EMCALI and other distributing companies
(subsidiaries of ICEL and CVC) serving a total of some 400,000 consumers.
CVC is administered by a 7-member Board of Directors and an Executive
Director appointed by the President of the Republic. CVC's various activi-
ties are managed as separate enterprises with independent accounting sys-
tems. CVC-CHIDRAL has received five fully-disbursed loans from the Bank
totalling MUS$44.6 (the last of which was made in 1963). It has an installed
capacity of 616 MW (571 MW hydro, 45 MW thermal). CVC's multipurpose
Salvajina project (intended mainly for flood control and irrigation), when
completed in 1984, will provide 270 MW of generating capacity and correspond-
inqg transmission facilities.
1.17      CORELCA.  An autonomous corporation, since 1972 CORELCA has been
responsible for power generation and transmission in 7 departments of the
Atlantic Coast. The 8 distributing companies in the area are subsidiaries of
CORELCA and buy power in bulk from it. Together they have an installed capa-
city of 766 MW (all thermal) and serve about 456,000 consumers. A 170 MW
steam station fueled by coal from El Cerrejon is to be commissioned in 1983,
and a second 170 mw unit is being installed for 1985 operation. CORELCA is
administered by a 7-member Board of Directors and a Managing Director. Its
management, organization and supervision of its subsidiaries have improved
considerably over the last 10 years. It has implemented satisfactorily
construction programs within its service area that included thermal plants
with units in the range of 150 M1, high voltage interconnecting transmission
lines, and distribution system expansion and works to improve its operating
efficiency.



1.18      ICEL.  A Government institute, ICEL reports to the Ministry of
Mines and Energy and provides electric power services largely to small towns
and rural areas of the country not served by the other main utilities. It
does so with the assistance of Government contributions through 13 subsidiary
companies in which it is the majority shareholder (the others are the respec-
tive local governments). ICEL's subsidiaries have an installed capacity of
663 MW (391 MW hydro, 272 MW thermal) and serve about 1,013,000 customers.
ICEL and some of its subsidiaries are expanding generation facilities, the
largest of which are the Betania hydro plant (for which a separate institu-
tion under ICEL's sponsorship has been created) which is to add 500 MW to the
national system in 1986-and the Tasajero thermal plant (150 MW) expected to
go into operation in 1984. ICEL is also responsible for the investment pro-
gram of most of its subsidiaries, comprising essentially expansion of the
various subtransmission and distribution systems and their connection to the
interconnected system, together with generating additions to isolated
systems. The projects carried out by ICEL are generally transferred to its
subsidiaries in the form of capital contributions. ICEL is administered by a
5-member Board of Directors (chaired by the Minister of Mines and Energy) and
a General Manager appointed by the President of the Republic. ICEL and its
subsidiaries have organizational and administrative problems, resulting from
frequent changes in middle management, low salaries, lack of qualified staff,
poor accounting systems and insufficient control by ICEL over the operations
of the geographically dispersed subsidiaries. A recently completed study on
organizational components by ICEL's Administrative Advisory Unit is expected
to initiate activities to resolve these problems (para. 4.12).
Planning and Coordination
1.19      The Ministry of Mines and Energy is charged with formulating
national policy for the generation, transmission and distribution of electri-
city, including coordination and supervision of power sector planning. It
shares with the National Social and Economic Policy Council (CONPES), DNP and
ISA responsibility for defining investment priorities. ISA defines a genera-
tion and transmission expansion program for the interconnected system; after
approval by the Ministry, DNP and CONPES, this becomes the National Power
Expansion Program. The Program is reviewed annually by ISA and revisions, if
necessary, are proposed. On the basis of requests from the companies, JNT in
DNP approves rate increases. Although the Government cannot enforce its
policies directly on the municipally controlled power companies, a mechanism
for reaching agreement on major issues affecting the sector is provided by
ISA, in which the Government has major holdings through ICEL and CORELCA.
Sector planning and coordination have improved markedly since the creation of
ISA, and the Bank has strongly supported this evolution through its lending.
National Least-Cost Power Expansion Program
1.20      Colombia has shared in the world-wide economic recession over the
last years and, over the same period, has effected upward adjustments in
electricity tariffs 44% in excess of the consumer price index (para. 1.23).
Taking account of the consequent slowing of electricity demand growth, on May
26, 1983, CONPES approved a revised National Power Expansion Program for
plants entering service during 1987-96 (Annex 1.2), representing a 31% reduc-
tion in investment expenditures previously planned during 1983-86. The plan
is based upon a phased resumption of the average annual economic growth rate



- 8 -
of 5.5% achieved from 1965-80, estimated fuel prices and the satisfying of a
conisequent projected 7.9% average annual electricity demand growth during the
17--year period 1983-2000. (Electricity demand growth has historically corre-
lated closely to overall economic growth at an elasticity ratio of 1.5.) For
the first time in Colombia, this plan also takes account of the probability
of construction delays based upon recent experience in constructing the coun-
try's first large hydro facilities. One-year delays are assumed in comple-
tion of all plants that will become operative after 1987 and provision is
made for maintaining a 95% reliability factor.2/ This plan would satisfac-
torily meet projected demand through year 2000.  During 1983, actual
electricity demand on the interconnected system rose by 7.2%, compared to the
1982 demand and to the 3.5% projected in the plan for the whole of 1983 over
the previous year. To ensure that significant variations in demand are
suitably taken into account, the Program should be annually reviewed and
updlated, as provided by CONPES. The next review is expected to be completed
by April 30, 1984. Thereafter, updating should be carried out in
coordination with the sector financing strategy review discussed in para.
5.13. Thus, during negotiations of the proposed loan, agreement was reached
that by September 30 of each year beginning in 1984, ISA would update, and
the Government would subsequently review together with the Bank, electricity
demand growth projections and the associated investment program. In support
of ISA's efforts to strengthen its planning methodology, a UNDP-financed
study, for which the Bank is the executing agency, has recently been
initiated, focusing mainly on demand forecasting and hydrological aspects.
The current Program adopted at the national level on May 26, 1983 has been
used as the basis for the financial projections in this report.
Power Market and Supply3!
1.21       Electric power has become the fastest-growing form of energy use in
Colombia. Its share of total energy consumption has almost doubled from 6.5%
in 1970 to 11.4% in 1980.  Generation increased by an average of about 10%
annually during 1970-1980, although it has slowed over the last years to 5.4%
per annum in 1981-82 (para 1.23). Annual per capita generation in 1982 was
about 750 kWh, which is below average for Latin America. Effective installed
capacity at the end of that year was 4,835 MW on the interconnected system
2/ In the unlikely event that all plants would be completed without the
one-year delay, the system would have the capacity to meet an 8.5%
average annual growth at a 95% reliability factor.
3/ Sources: ICEL "La Electrificacion en Colombia 1980-1981," ISA, "Energy

by CONPES May 1983. Actual satistics shown in this report are through
December 31, 1982. Annual data from 1983 onward are those projected in
ISA's "Revised Expansion Plan".



- 9 -
and 5,077 MW including self-producers, indicating the public entities
accounting for 95% of the total installed capacity and generation.
Diversified peak demand was about 3,854 MW, and total energy generation in
1982 amounted to 21,525 GWh, of which 21,300 GWh was supplied by ISA and its
shareholders. Hydro plants accounted for about 69% of the total energy
generation.
1.22      About 54% of Colombia's 26.7 million population has electric power,
compared to 26% in 1950 and 45% in 1970. The urban population, comprising
about 68% of the population, has greater access. In 1976, for example, 90%
of households in large cities (population of 50,000 or more) had electrical
service while in rural towns (population between 500-2,500) the corresponding
figure was 36%; in other rural areas, 13%. Various programs including the
Bank-financed First and Second Integrated Rural Development Projects and the
Village Electrification Project as well as programs financed by IDB,
Kreditanstalt fur Wiederaufbau (KfW) and the Colombia Coffee Growers Associa-
tion, are aimed at increasing rural coverage (see also para. 1.09). In 1982
there were about 2.8 million electricity subscribers of which 88.5% were
residential and 9.2% commercial. Electricity sales in 1982 totalled about
16,708 GWh4/. Consumption by consumer category is shown in Annex 1.3.
1.23      During 1970-1982 gross generation increased at an average annual
rate of about 9.2%, compared to the 10% annual average during the 1970s
(Annex 1.3). The slower generation growth rate was brought about by economic
recession in 1981 that affected adversely all economic sectors in Colombia,
compounded by drought conditions that reduced generation from the hydro
plants and resulted in consequent power restrictions and brownouts in the
cities. Tariff increases on the order of 15-20% above the consumer price
index throughout the sector in that year may have further suppressed demand.
Growth of total sales decreased proportionally. As mentioned previously, the
new National Power Expansion Program takes account of these factors. The
system demand forecast for total gross generation for 1983-1990 anticipated a
rise from 23.1 TWh to 38.8 TWh (Annex 1.4). Demand for gross generation was
forecast to grow at about 7.9% on average annually, with the system load
factor expected to remain constant at 64% during the period. Maximum system
MW demand is expected to increase from 4,120 MW in 1983 to 6,921 in 1990,
reflecting an average annual increase of about 7.7%. System losses were
assumed at about 20% of losses.5/ The installation of about 5,318 MW is
planned during 1983-92 to meet forecast demands. System load factor is
expected to remain constant during the period at 64%.
Recent Developments
1.24      During the past three years the sector has suffered financial
problems caused mainly by lower than expected revenues resulting from tariff
adjustment delays, slow demand growth, rationing, and higher than expected
local cost shares of investments as well as insufficient availability of
financing. Local borrowings needed by the sector have outpaced the domestic
4f Includes minor international sales to Ecuador and Venezuela.
5J Discrepancies that exist between the sales forecasts and those prepared
by each of the companies result from the usage of differing loss
percentages and other adjustments made after the Energy Balance had been
published by ISA.



- 10 -
banking system's lending capability. Moreover, Government-related lending
earmarked for the sector was held back and monetization of external borrow-
ings was authorized only on a highly selective basis.
1.25      As a result, when the current national administration took office
in August 1982 the sector's financing deficit for the year was estimated to
amount to US$430 million and the previously-favorable lending climate for
Colombia in the international capital market became affected by developments
in other Latin American countries and began to deteriorate. In the Fall of
1982, the authorities moved quickly to review the sector financing plan and
to authorize badly-needed tariff increases for EEEB (a 22% lump increase and
3.25% monthly, amounting to 73% during the next 12 months) and EPM (a 20%
one-time adjustment and continuance of 2.2% a month, or 52% over the same
period) and to adopt a financing program to cover the sector's 1982 short-
fall, as well as the afcre-mentioned new borrowings needed in 1983-84.  In
doing so, the authorities have also recognized the constraint on sound sector
development and finances represented by the 12 mostly rural-based local uti-
lities loosely grouped under the holding company, ICEL. The Government,
therefore, confirmed explicitly the need to restructure the ICEL-group
(para. 5.08) and also to take special measures, still to be defined, to
assist the Atlantic Coast regional company, CORELCA, in strengthening the
eight coastal utilities under its wing. (The one negative development on
this score was a short-lived countrywide tariff freeze, in late 1982, follow-
ing civil disturbances associated with electricity charges to consumers and
quality of service.) Further, studies were initiated on the possiblity of
inter-regional unification of tariff structures over the long term. In addi-
tion to tariff measures, the authorities took other decisive actions to mobi-
lize needed local resources. Legislative and other regulatory measures were
enacted to put the new financial arm of the power sector, FEN (Chapter 2), on
an operational footing. As a result of a well-orchestrated effort by the
Government, FEN and private sector managers in the financial system, FEN
attracted considerable private financial savings in its initial placement
(para. 2.13), and the first loans to the power companies have been finalized.
1.26      Through the exercise of defining the sectoral financing program,
for the first time in recent years the Government began to come to grips with
the macroeconomic and institutional implications of the 1983-90 power invest-
ment program.  In May 1983, it reviewed in detail the justification for, and
financial feasibility of all new investments in the aforementioned program,
taking account of competing demands from other priority sectors, with conse-
quent reduction by almost one-third of planned capital outlays for electri-
city (para. 1.20). The new authorities have thus set a course of managing
more directly the impact of electricity development on the rest of the eco-
nomv through careful assessment of proposed investment programs in terms of
economic and technical soundness, compatibility with overall public invest-
ment: expenditure plans, and financial feasibility at the national and
sectoral level.  This approach would help avoid the severe cash flow
difficulties of recent years. Moreover, it would bring under regular
scrutiny those investments undertaken by individual companies that do not



come under the aegis of the National Power Expansion Program (which includes
generation plant and transmission lines, but not subtransmission or distribu-
tion), thereby contributing to the efficiency of resource allocation. The
proposed project would support this evolution by helping to mobilize
resources and assisting the Government to review on a regular basis the
justification for new investments, the sector's financial performance and
investment capability (paras. 2.28-2.29, 4.08 and 5.13). During negotia-
tions, assurance was received that new electricity development projects would
be authorized by the Government only if the project in question would be eco-
nomically justified and there would be available adequate financing that
would not affect negatively the existing financial obligations of the
company(ies) concerned.
Bank Participation
1.27      Since 1950, the Banik has made 27 loans to Colombia's power sector,
totalling US$1,374 million (Annex 1.5). In addition, the Bank is assisting
with the preparation of three generation and transmission projects now at the
feasibility stage: Rio Grande Multipurpose project (water supply and power,
EPM), Calima III Hydro Power (CVC) and Urra (Alto Sinu) Hydro Power (CORELCA
and ISA). Past loans have assisted the expansion of generating capacity, and
transmission and distribution facilities in the systems serving Bogota,
Medellin, Cali, Cartagena, Bucaramanga and Manizales, including expansion of
electricity distribution to low income areas (874-CO, 1973; 1807-CO, 1980;
and 1868-CO, 1980). The Bogota Distribution Project (1807-CO, 1981) has been
the first Bank loan to Colombia to support exclusively distribution expansion
and, in addition to other beneficiaries, would directly benefit about 340,000
lower income consumers. In addition, the Bank has supported rural electrifi-
cation under 246-CO (1960) and 313-CO (1962), the First and Second Integrated
Rural Development Project (1352-CO, 1977; 2174-CO, 1983), and the Village
Electrification Project (1999-CO, 1983). Other recent loans included the
500-kV Interconnection Line (1583-CO, 1978), and for hydro power generation,
the San Carlos I and II (Loans 1582-CO, 1978; 1725-CO, 1979), Mesitas
(1628-CO, 1979), Guadalupe IV (1868-CO, 1980), Playas (1953-CO, 1981) and
Guavio Projects (2008-CO, 1982) which would, respectively, complete the task
of interconnecting the country's regional power systems begun under 575-CO
(1968), and add a total of 3,253 MW of capacity to the national interconnect-
ed system. In addition to coordinating a technical assistance project to
strengthen system planning (para. 1.20), the Bank is the executing agency for
another UNDP-financed project which would enhance the sector's construction
management capability for large hydroelectric facilities. Further, in
connection with Bank lending, marginal cost tariff studies have been carried
out for the major markets by ISA's shareholders. The adaption of the results
of the study into the various tariff structures is under discussion with the
Government. Taken together, the above-mentioned projects reflect the Bank's
participation in overall power development in Colombia, from the planning
stage through financing and construction of generation and transmission faci-
lities, to delivery of service to the final consumer. Finally, as part of
its continuing dialogue with the Government and the sector, the Bank is plan-
ning a power sector memorandum for 1984 which would address the full energy
balance and expansion planning (para. 1.20), load dispatch, power losses
(para. 1.23), sector organization (para. 1.25), salaries and training, and
financing strategy (para. 5.13).



- 12 -
1. 28     Previous Bank lending to Colombia's power sector has been found
generally successful in several OED reports. For instance, the most recent
report, "Power Interconnection (575-CO) and Chivor Hydroelectric Projects
(681-CO)" (Report No. 2720, October 29, 1979), commented upon the Bank's
participation in Colombia's effort to evolve a stronger and more efficient
power sector organization. Through the creation of ISA in conjunction with
these projects, and the steps taken to overcome financial and institutional
difficulties, real progress was made toward more coordinated sector develop-
ment. Despite implementation delays and increased costs, both projects were
successfully implemented. Also, the report entitled "Bank Operations in
Colombia, an Evaluation" (Report No. Z-18) of May 25, 1972, concluded that
Bank financing was successful in assisting the power companies to develop
hydroelectric plants at lower unit cost than they otherwise would have been
able to do. In turn, this permitted greater urban coverage as well as
cheaper and more reliable electricity supply to industry. The report com-
mended Bank efforts in the establishment of JNT and the central intercon-
nected system, which facilitated further power sector development. Among
other things, the report recommended that in the future the Bank pay
increased attention to the companies' financial planning, tariff structures,
distribution programs and energy losses. These points have been addressed
under the aforementioned recent loans, which have been the first Bank opera-
tions since late 1972 because sector financial difficulties precluded needed
expansion of generation facilities in the interim. Complementary measures to
sustain investment capability, and thus ensure completion of high priority
projects in a reasonable period, and to strengthen financial planning are a
main focus of the proposed project.



- 13 -
2. THE BORROWER, THE FINANCIERA ELECTRICA NACIONAL
Introduction
2.01      Given the structure and relatively small size of Colombia's
financial institutions (Chapter 3), by 1981 it had become evident that the
rapid growth over the previous three years of the power sector's requirements
for medium and long-term local currency financing could not be adequately
met. Further, the large volume of resources involved also implied a need for
a specialized institution to oversee the sector's finances, coordinate an
overall financing strategy and monitor the economic efficiency with which
loan proceeds were used. In conjunction with the Guavio Hydro Power Project
(2008-CO), the Government confirmed its intention to set up such an
institution. In an important elaboration of the original concept, the
Colombian authorities decided to empower the new entity to raise capital in
external as well as local market to enable concentrating on substantial share
of overall resource mobilization for the sector, with attendant economies.
Thus, FEN was established by Law 11 of January 1982 and Decree 1471 of May
1982, as a financial entity of the State with a share capital subscribed by
the Nation and, initially, six power companies operating at the national,
departmental and municipal levels. FEN is encharged with:
(a)  structuring and coordinating power sector finances;
(b)  making medium and long-term loans (1 to 20 years) exclusively to
its shareholding electric utilities;
(c)  mobilizing domestic savings by financial paper issues and other in-
struments after obtaining authorization from the National Monetary
Board;
(d)  mobilizing external resources, subject to the pertinent regulations
pertaining to public entities; and
(e)  administering for its shareholding electric utilities bond issues
and trusts, and guarantee, agent or payment contracts, and opening
letters of credit.
Although FEN is an independent financial institution, both the Government and
the sector companies participate in its policy decisions. Through exercise
of its mandate, FEN is expected to play an important role, not only in
providing financing but also in extending timely guidance to the utilities on
financial management issues and in advising the Government on national policy
matters related to the sector's ability to carry out high priority
investments. Toward this end, under the proposed project FEN will carry out
annually a review of sector finances and investment financing strategy
(para. 5.13).
Legal Status
2.02      FEN's legal status is sui generis, that is, although it is under
the general jurisdiction of the Superintendency of Banks, its characteristics
and regulatory framework are those specifically assigned to it. By virtue of



- 14 -
the role it is to play, this unique status is necessary.  For example, FEN is
the only state enterprise in Colombia devoted to providing finance to a sub-
group of its shareholders; the only rediscount development bank outside the
Central Bank (Banco de la Republica, BR); the only bank whose lending activi-
ties are restricted to a single sector composed of public companies; and it
is one of the largest financial institutions in the country, with a capital
base equivalent to about 20% of the total banking system. Therefore, FEN
requires a suitably tailored control system to avoid conflict of interest,
and to provide appropriate liquidity, solvency and operating parameters.
This has been provided for (paras. 2.27-2.29), although further refinements
may be introduced as experience is gained (para. 2.03).
Organization, Administration and Personnel
2.03      FEN's main shareholders comprise the National Government, ISA,
EEEB, EPM, CVC, ICEL, CHEC, and CORELCA, who form its General Assembly
(para. 2.08). It is managed by a Board of Directors and a General Manager.
The Board is chaired by the Minister of Mines and Energy and its remaining
members are the Chief of the National Planning Department, the General
Manager of the BR and three representatives of the power company sharehold-
ers. The General Manager is elected by the Board for a two-year, renewable
term. Provision is also made for an independent auditor, elected by the
General Assembly on a biannual, renewable basis (para. 2.06). These arrange-
ments are satisfactory. FEN's Bylaws cover the important aspects of the
entity's role and operations and are acceptable; changes require executive
Government approval. Any change in the legal framework by which FEN was set
up or in its Bylaws, that would have a material and adverse effect upon the
entity, would constitute an event of default under the proposed A-loan.
2.04      The General Manager, an economist and former Dean of Economics at
Universidad Externado de Colombia, credit manager of Caja Agraria, and
ministrial advisor, took up his duties in 1982 and has made strong progress
in internal organization, personnel recruitment, public relations, initial
mobilization of financial savings and in general assessment of the new insti-
tution's role and policies. FEN's organizational plan (Chart) provides for a
small staff of professionally-qualified people, initially about 20.  Core
professional staff of about 25 are in place and are well qualified.
Permanent staff will be complemented by consultants, at least in the legal
and tax fields. The organization is divided into five areas:
(a)  General Secretariat:  includes the legal and public relations func-
tions (e.g., coordination with the Government and power sector);
(b)  Administration:  responsible for the management of non-financial
assets and personnel;
(c)  Finance:  covers planning and budgeting, treasury and portfolio
management, accounting and marketing;
(d)  Technical:  responsible for loans and rediscounts, and sector
analysis and studies, such as forecasting and monitoring of finan-
cial performance of the FEN and the electric sector, formulating
financial policy and action proposals for the Government and for
the sector; and



- 15 -
(e) External Operations: charged with all matters related to foreign
financial transactions.
2.05      FEN's organization is well suited to its objectives, and its con-
cept of a small, qualified professional group supported by outside consul-
tants would favor efficiency. FEN has recognized that there are two areas in
which additional staff or temporary consultants with permanent staff members
as counterparts, would be useful:
(a) to enable FEN to administer efficiently the proposed project, and
to strengthen its technical capacity in evaluating loan proposals
and in project monitoring. Toward this end, FEN has agreed to
engage by June 30, 1984, the services of consultants with qualifi-
cations and experience satisfactory to the bank. In addition to
providing technical assistance to FEN's existing staff, the consul-
tants will also carry out, by December 31, 1984, and under terms of
reference satisfactory to the Bank, a study of FEN's technical
staffing requirements. Based upon the study's recommendations and
the Bank's comments, FEN would employ the additional experienced
and qualified staff indicated; and
(b) to design the FEN's operating procedures, including the techniques
of systems analysis, down to the level of flow charts, to facili-
tate its later computerization. Personnel are also needed to
determine the hardware and main software for FEN's computer facili-
ties. FEN needs to be able to process not only its own operations
and financial planning but also the cash flow for power projects,
and historical and projected financial statements of the electric
utilities and of the sector as a whole. For this purpose, FEN has
recently employed 3 professional staff, including the former
Director of Systems Analysis of DNP, as well as assistant-level
staff. FEN will proceed with installation of computer facilities
compatible with those of the power companies as soon as possible
(para. 4.08).
Accounting and Auditing
2.06      FEN is in process of organizing its internal accounting and audit-
ing offices. Initial evidence of their work is positive. During negotiation
of the proposed loan it was confirmed that systems compatible with sound
principles will be maintained. FEN has also agreed to retain the services of
independent, external auditors satisfactory to the Bank, in line with the
usual Bank requirements.
Financial Operations
2.07      Sources of Funds.  FEN has four main sources of funds:  its own
capital, official funds, and resources obtained from the domestic and exter-
nal capital markets, together with retained earnings. During negotiations,
agreement was reached that FEN will annually capitalize its profits.

By far the largest source of FEN's capital is the contribution of COL$9,691

corresponding to the discount value of the Electricity Development Fund's
(FDE) outstanding credits. The accrual of these monies to FEN was estimated
at Col$1,300 million in 1982, Col$3,591 million in 1983, and Col$4,900
million in 1984. (As of December 31, 1983, Col$5,095.4 million had been
received by FEN). By December 31, 1983, in excess of Col$10,300 million of
the subscribed capital had been paid. FEN's actual and projected net worth
and capital resources through 1987 are shown in Annexes 2.3 and 2.4. Net
worth is projected to increase from Col$10,148 million in 1982 to Col$30,595
million in 1987, or about 25% per annum on average.
2.09       From the operational point of view, FEN's capital provides a sub-
stantial amount of funds at zero financial cost to FEN. It also sets the
ceiling to the entity's obligations with the public which, as set out in its
Bylaws, may not exceed 20 times its paid-in capital plus legal reserves. To
protect its capital from being eroded by inflation, avoid a net transfer of
resources from the power sector to the Government and enable FEN to lend to
the power companies at reasonable rates, legislation has been enacted to
exempt the entity's income from taxation.
2.10       Official Funds.  In past years virtually all the medium-to-long
term local borrowings available to the power sector were based upon official
funds (para. 3.20) channelled through the now-defunct Electricity Development
Fund (FDE) of BR. To provide some continued access to such funds, Law 11, by
1/  In June 1983, FEN's General Assembly of Shareholders voted to increase
its share capital by 600 units. 553 of the new shares have been divided
among the electrificadoras of ICEL (182 shares) and CORELCA (126), Emcali
(45), and Central Hidroelectrica de Betania (200), S.A., thus including
Colombia's remaining power companies among FEN shareholders and admitting
them to national-level sectoral discussions, albeit appropriately as
minor shareholders. The other 47 new shares have been subscribed by the
founding shareholders, comprising 27 by ISA and 4 each by EEEB, CVC,
CORELCA, ICEL and CHEC.

which FEN was established, provided that the entity receive 30%2 of the
proceeds of the Social Security Institute's (ISS) Constant Value Bonds that
were previously managed by the Industrial Development Institute (IFI) to
provide financing for goods and services provided locally. These monies,
which may be used for the same purpose by FEN's shareholders, accrue monthly
to FEN, and are to be repaid over 25 years at 5.5 percentage points over
their inflation-adjusted value. Since 1979 the amount of these funds managed
by IFI has more than doubled, reaching Col$2,328 million in 1982; FEN is
expected to receive about Col$7,301 million from this source over 1984-87
(Annex 2.5). In addition, by resolution of the Monetary Board, BR has been
authorized to invest Col$3,000 million in FEN paper, which is expected to be
effected in 1984, and the funds reinvested as they mature.
Also, CONPES has recommended that FEN receive 4% of the annual increase of
incremental legal reserves in BR's credit certificates, which had previously
been directed to the power sector through the FDE; however, this is expected
to comprise only a minor source of funds (Annex 2.5).
2.11      Domestic Capital Market.  Local investors are to be a main source
of funding for FEN.  To tap this market, FEN is empowered, inter alia, to
emit bonds, sell promissory notes, term certificates of deposit, and other
financial paper, and place shares and bonds issued by power companies.
Although the financing requirements of the power sector are mainly for
medium- and long-term loans, current tax regulations and the inflationary
environment do not favor the raising of medium- and long-term funds in the
domestic capital market.
2.12      Given Colombian investors' strong orientation toward short-term
paper, it may take some time for FEN to be able to attract substantial
mediumr and long-term investments, even if there would be favorable tax
treatment. In the interim, this implies a considerable reliance on term
transformation, i.e., borrowing short and lending long. However, the
FEN has not yet been provided with access to short-term borrowing from BR to
cushion it against an unexpected drop in savings mobilization, similar to
that available to the commercial and development banks, and savings and loan
associations. To ensure that an adequate quota would be timely available, it
should be assigned prior to loan effectiveness in an amount and on terms and
conditions satisfactory to the Bank. An acceptable arrangement would be to
include FEN among the financial institutions covered by Resolution 72 of
December 1, 1982 of the Monetary Board. This resolution provides a quota up
to 100% of paid-in capital and legal reserves, for a maximum of 90 days
annually, at 27% interest.
2.13      Until local conditions imply a reasonable probability of marketing
medium and long-term financial instruments, FEN will rely on short-term
instruments and, at least initially, will follow closely the design of paper
issued successfully in the recent past. FEN has been authorized to place and
maintain in circulation through end-1984 Col$7,000 million (US$88 million) in
Electricity Certificates (CEVs), representing its first financial issue.
Between April 20, 1983, the first day of the offering and end-August, the
CEVs were fully sold, 30% in 90-day and 70% in 180-day paper.  It was
marketed through the Bogota Stock Exchange and commercial banks, with
liquidity fund management provided by the Fiduciaria Bursatil, S.A., sold at
discount and in multiples of Col$100,000 (US$1,252). The average effective
return to investors at maturity is 33.48%, i.e., about 13.5 percentage points



- 18 -
above 1984 estimated inflation. As this issue has matured, FEN has been able
to replace it entirely in 180-day paper.  These favorable results may be
attributed to FEN's having chosen a proven financial instrument and having
projected a strong professional and financial image in the business and
financial community. Timing was another factor. Because of the priority
accorded to electricity investment financing, the Monetary Board had
authorized FEN to enter the market at a time when the pricing of other
available issues was relatively less competitive. Thus, estimates of FEN's
future ability to mobilize finance take account of the rather special market
conditions that prevailed during the entity's first venture (para. 2.30 and
Annex 2.6).
2.14      FEN has recently requested authorization from the National Monetary
Board to issue an additional Col$15,000 million, two-thirds in CEVs and
one-third in a two-year bond with an upward sliding return for investors who
hold it beyond a minimum period. This is directly responsive to a Monetary
Board recommendation that FEN investigate innovative mechanisms to attract
resources from the financial sector.
2.15      FEN is also studying other options for future placements.  One of
these is a medium-term bond with a floating interest rate (adjustable
quarterly by an index of the previous three-month prevailing rate),
three-year maturity, a liquidity (not support) fund, and either partial
capitalization of interest (by establishing a set interest rate at the outset
and capitalizing the rest of it) or amortization of nominal principal in
three annual installments). Another possibility is a medium-term fixed rate
bonad with provision for monetary correction, (Annex 2.1).2/
2.16      Another option, for a later stage, may be a mediumrterm bond
yielding the rate of devaluation plus some interest. This would compete with
the Transferrable Certificates of Exchange. Since the latter are issued for
six- and twelve-month terms, the new paper could differentiate itself with
maturities of about two years and a somewhat higher interest rate. However,
the tax treatment of nominal interest earned would govern the feasibility of
these bonds, as well as Variable Interest Rate Bonds (BIVs).3/ To promote
progress toward a deepening of market receptivity, it was agreed during
negotiations that, by December 31, 1984, FEN would prepare and furnish to the
Government and the Bank a plan of action acceptable to them for encouraging
medium- and long-term private investment in FEN paper.  The Government has
ag;reed that it would promptly thereafter put such plan into action.
2/ In Colombia there are papers of this type, the housing mortgage bonds
(UPACs), which receive a favorable tax treatment, annual interest earned
exempt up to 8%; from 8% to the rate of monetary correction, interest is
taxable at one-half the otherwise applicable tax rate, and only above the
latter does the full rate apply. UPACs have two drawbacks: monetary
correction has been less than inflation, and even their preferred tax
treatment does not compete strongly with short-term paper. Nevertheless,
even to issue bonds similar to the UPAC's requires legal authorization.
3/ An additional obstacle to issuing BIVs is the lack of a free index of
short-term interest rates.



- 19 -
2.17      Total resources that could be mobilized by FEN from local investors
are projected to grow from Col$18,500 million in 1984 to Col$62,900 million
in 1987 (paras. 3.24-3.27 and Annex 2.6), although market uncertainties
preclude disaggregating this estimate between short- and longer-term
resources. To help ensure that FEN mobilizes domestic savings at a level
consistent with its maintaining an important share in incremental financial
resources, agreement has been reached during negotiations on annual targeted
amounts of funds to be raised from local investors (Annex 2.7). These
targets would be reviewed annually by FEN, the Government and the Bank, and
updated with Bank concurrence. Also during negotiations, assurance has been
obtained from the Government that it would take all measures necessary on its
part to provide FEN with adequate access to the domestic capital market.
External Capital Market
2.18      FEN's external operations may include borrowings, bond placements,
export credits on behalf of the power utilities and other lines of credit,
subject to the normal regulations pertaining to public entities. Foreign
borrowing requires the prior approval of DNP (and CONPES, in the case of debt
guaranteed by the Republic), the Ministry of Finance and the Monetary Board,
who coordinate public sector borrowing, confirm its compatibility with
national policy and supervise the terms and conditions of borrowing. FEN
would assist in this by providing company and sector-wide financial informa-
tion relating to such proposed borrowing.
2.19      Although FEN's mandate envisages borrowings in the international
capital market on behalf of the power companies, it was planned that the
individual utilities would continue to secure all their own external capital
requirements during the early years of FEN's operations. However, present
market uncertainties vis-a-vis Latin America have cast some doubt upon the
several utilities' access to external commercial sources for the financing
needed during 1984-85.  Given the volume of ongoing and future electricity
investments and the number of utilities involved, the concept of concentrat-
ing an important part of resource mobilization in a specialized intermediary
offers advantages in terms of efficiency and economies of time and cost.
Instead of the companies having to stagger sequentially, for strategical
reasons, their forays into the capital market, FEN may go to the market on
behalf of their consolidated requriements, thereby permitting a smoother cash
flow for the companies and helping to diminish the cost of late payments to
contractors, and others. Despite its recent creation, FEN is by far the
largest financial entity in the country in terms of net worth. It has
already chalked up a notable success in the domestic market, and the Governr-
ment has not only strongly supported its establishment but is also its main
shareholder. The proposed Bank and cofinancing loans would assist in intro-
ducing FEN to the external market. At the same time, it would be inadvisable
to consider directing all lending to the sector through FEN, principally
because the primary financing and the appraisal requirements of large, com-
plex projects can be more effectively handled directly with the utility
involved.



- 20 -
Lending Operations
2.20       FEN's borrowers, its power company shareholders, are discussed in
Chapter 1, and their funding requirements in Chapter 5. FEN's Credit Commit-
tee, whose members are the representatives of BR, Ministry of Mines and
Energy, DNP, the President of FEN and one representative of the power sector,
considers loan applications from the power company shareholders. Upon the
Committee's favorable recommendation, the proposed loan is presented for
final approval by FEN's Board of Directors. The composition of the Credit
Committee is satisfactory because the parties involved would represent a
balanced basis for decision-making (para. 2.28). Further, a favorable
recommendation to the Board for loan approval would also require a positive
vote of the representative of either BR or DNP.
2.21       FEN has two ways of lending in the usual course of its business.
First, a local bank, with FEN's authorization, lends to a power company based
upon prior agreement with FEN that the bank will in effect borrow from FEN a
high percentage of the face loan amount to be lent to the power company. In
practice, these are rediscounted loans. Secondly, FEN may make direct loans
to individual power companies, which must be secured with local bank
guarantees. In addition, special arrangements have been worked out for
handling the proceeds of external loans (para. 2.27).
2.22       Indirect (Rediscounted) Loans.  Because FEN will conduct its lend-
ing of domestically-based resources through the local banking system, the
system's capability to handle the projected volume of lending is paramount.
In this respect, the dominant issue has been the limits to which banks may
lend to individual borrowers. Article 86(1) of Law 45 of 1923 provided that
no bank could lend to an individual borrower if his total outstanding obliga-
tions to that bank amounts to 10% of the bank's paid-in capital and reserves,
unless the borrower would provide the bank with real security valued commer-
cially at 120% of the value of the obligations guaranteed by such security,
in which case the amount of the borrower's total obligations could not exceed
25% of the bank's paid-in capital and reserves. Moreover, by Decree 3233 of
1965 it was established that, for purposes of calculating the individual
credit limits, the guarantees granted by the bank to the borrower should also
be taken into account. The 10% and 15% ceilings authorized left little room
for new lending to the major power companies, without their giving liens on
assets. In essence, this problem derived from the lopsided relationship
between a banking system in a modest stage of development and a group of
electricity companies with a comparatively large base of income-earning fixed
assets and, thus, debt service capability. In other words, the regulations
did not address the creditworthiness of the borrower and sought only to avoid
concentration of risk. In April 1983, with Bank support, the lending ceil-
ings in respect of the power sector were raised to 25% of total capital and
reserves including revaluation for unsecured loans and 40%, with a pledge of
real security. As discussed below, the new limit on unsecured lending appear
generally ample to allow sufficient local funds to reach the sector through
1987.
2.23       Assuming no reduction of levels of the power companies' indebted-
ness, the new legal limits would exceed by a wide margin, the local borrow-
ings projected by each power company during 1983-87, and would be sufficient
to accommodate the proposed A and B-loans. Annexes 2.7-2.10 show the present



- 21 -
and projected level of the domestic banking system's capital and legal
reserves over this period, and local indebtedness of the sector and of each
company. During 1983-85 approximately US$1,164 million equivalent of legal
local borrowing capability would not have been used by the six major utili-
ties (Annex 2.10), based upon the consolidated sector financial projections.
In respect of the four main companies to be included as sub-borrowers under
the proposed A and B-loans (para. 4.08), the surplus borrowing scope would
amount to an estimated US$701 million equivalent during 1983-85, which indi-
cates that the banking system would be able to accomodate legally the pro-
posed US$370 million external loans without crowding out the anticipated flow
of domestic resources to the companies. (The implications of the FEN's own
lending ceiling are discussed in para. 2.26).
2.24      Aside from the matter of legal lending limits, there is the ques-
tion of whether there would be adequate incentives for local banks to parti-
cipate in rediscounting operations. In principle, banks would be expected to
do so if their profit margins would equal or exceed alternative uses of their
funds. When FEN authorizes an indirect loan to a power company, it will
rediscount most of the loan given by a bank to a company (i.e., about 90%).
Normally in making loans, banks would be required by the Government to invest
an additional amount (16.5%) in Agricultural Fiduciary Fund (FFA) paper that
gives an annual yield of about 8%, well below the market interest rate. To
assist FEN in being able to offer attractive margins to the banks without
raising the interest rate charged to its borrowers, or increasing the dis-
counted share of the loan (which would reduce the flow of funds to the
sector), the Monetary Board has exempted FEN's rediscount operations from FFA
forced investments. With this action, the banks will be able to obtain an
attractive yield, about 36% (about 14% in real terms) on their own resources,
enabling FEN initially to charge the banks an annual rediscount rate of 27%
paid quarterly in advance and maintain a rediscount margin of about 90%, and
the power companies would be charged annual interest of 28% p.q.a. (repre-
senting an effective interest rate of 32.3%).
2.25      Direct Loans.  As mentioned above, direct loans from FEN require
bank guarantees and the present cost of such guarantees would be at least
0.5%-1.0% annually on the outstanding balance. However, in direct lending
local banks would not provide additionality of funds to the sector, and
guarantees are counted against regulated bank lending limits at 120% of the
face value of the loan.   Consequently, FEN intends to use the rediscount
mechanism as its usual lending channel.
2.26      Just as commercial bank lending is subject to ceilings, direct and
indirect FEN loans are, too. Under the original statutory limits, FEN could
authorize loans, without real security, only up to an amount equivalent to
25% of its capital and legal reserves to an individual company, and up to 50%
with such security. As shown in Annex 2.11, FEN's unsecured outstanding
lending to each power company could not have exceeded a total of Col$2,822
million through 1985, equivalent to about US$38 million during 1983-87. In
January 1984, the Government took account of the various inconsistencies in
restricting thusly FEN's lending capability. First, FEN's estimated capabil-
ity to mobilize funds during 1983-87 would far exceed its lending capability
in relation to the six major-investing power companies. Secondly, the limits
would take no account of borrowers' creditworthiness. Furthermore, in any
event, FEN would not ultimately bear the credit risk of lending locally-



- 22 -
raised to the power companies; this would always be borne by local banks.
Third, FEN's real risk would lie in its exposure to individual local commer-
cial banks, which was not then regulated. Finally, the composition of FEN's
Credit Committee, which approves loan requests, would reduce to a low proba-
bility possible undue concentration of lending in any individual company.
Prior to negotiation of the proposed project, the Government amended FEN's
statutory framework to resolve this.
2.27      Decree No. 3574 of December 30, 1983 provides that the onlending to
the power companies of loans deriving from external sources would not be sub-
ject to lending ceilings on the commercial banks or FEN; further the guaran-
tees specified in Law 11 could be obtained not only from local banks, but
also the Government. The Decree also provides that FEN's legal lending
limits in respect of local resources are to be calculated on the basis of
FEN's total capital and reserves.
Credit Policy and Regulations
2.28      Because FEN's lending activities will influence in an important way
the economic efficiency of electricity investments, it is essential that its
credit policy and regulations provide an appropriate framework. Its lending
would be project-based, within a contractual framework that (a) calls for the
power company borrowers to achieve/maintain a sound level of managerial,
operational and financial performance; (b) specifies conditions governing
procurement and disbursement to ensure that proceeds are used in an economic-
ally efficient manner and for specified purposes; (c) provides for causes of
suspension of disbursements and acceleration of loan maturity; and (d)
requires regular reporting, as well as post-project evaluation. FEN's
interest rate policy ties variable interest rate to its total cost of funds
plus a reasonable profit and provides that exchange rate risk in external
loans is borne by the ultimate borrower (para. 4.09).
2.29      The Bank has reviewed and discussed with FEN its credit policy and
manual of regulations and operational procedures. Consultant services pro-
vided under the project would, under terms of reference acceptable to the
Bank, assist FEN in improving further upon its manual of operational proce-
dures to help ensure an efficient operation. Agreement has been reached that
changes which, in the Bank's opinion, would have an adverse effect on FEN or
the proposed project, would not be introduced into the credit policy or regu-
lations.
Financial Projections
2.30      At this stage, financial projections can be made only in a highly
summarized form based upon estimates of the entity's total local resource
mobilization capability, total annual lending to the power companies, costs
of funds and operations, and the basic financial performance parameters that
would be adhered to. On this basis, FEN's financial outlook is favorable
(Annexes 2.12-2.16), its net worth is projected to grow threefold during
1982-1987, from Col$10,148 million to Col$30,595 million. Estimated sources
and uses of funds during this period are:

2.31       In view of the importance of the official funds to FEN's sources of
financing, a change in the statutory provision for them that would negatively
affect FEN's financial condition would be a condition of default under the
proposed loan. It can be observed that FEN's liquidity would need to be
monitored carefully given the entity's reliance upon term transformation and
the risk of collection delays. During negotiations, agreement was reached
that FEN will not authorize new lending to any borrower which is in default
under any existing loan granted by FEN either as a direct or rediscounted
loan (see also para. 2.28). Further, confirmation was received that FEN will
maintain at all times a liquidity buffer equivalent to not less than two
months of estimated annual expenses plus one month of the estimated amount of
principal that would become due and payable to local investors during the
year; these estimates would be agreed during the annual review discussed
below. Additionally, FEN would maintain a debt-equity ratio not to exceed
7:1, based upon all indebtedness. It was also agreed that FEN, the Govern-
ment and the Bank will review annually, beginning in March 1984, the entity's
domestic resource mobilization (para. 2.23), financial performance and pros-
pects; this will be carried out in light of the annual power sector financing
strategy review discussed in paras. 1.20 and 5.13.
