3. THE FINANCIAL SECTOR
Trends in the Colombian Financial Sector
3.01      Institutional Structure.  Colombia has a relatively well-developed
financial sector made up of highly specialized institutions. It comprises
the BR, about 25 each of commercial banks and corporaciones financieras
(CFs--investment banks), a central mortgage bank (Banco Central
Hipotecario--BCH), 10 savings and loan associations (Corporaciones de Ahorro
y Vivienda--CAVIs), nearly 40 trade finance companies (Companias de
Financiamiento Comercial (CFCs), more than 70 insurance companies, several
mutual funds and three stock exchanges (at Bogota, Medellin and Cali). The
commercial banks have the largest branch network, with more than 1,800
offices distributed throughout the country, followed by the CAVIs (400 branch
offices in total), BCH (75 offices) and CFs (over 100 branch offices). (Annex
3.1)
3.02      Developments Through 1972.  The Colombian financial sector was
relatively repressed through the end of 1972. Financial savings were
depressed by low ceilings on interest rates, reserve requirements were high,
and credit was to a large extent rationed. By the end of the 1960s, an
increasingly flourishing informal (non-banking) market had developed,
reflecting attempts to circumvent the regulations of the formal financial
system.
Although the process of financial liberalization did not start
until the end of 1972, a number of measures taken in the late 196Cs and early
1970s laid the foundations for such a liberalization. The establishment of a
crawling peg exchange rate system, in 1967, reduced the risk of abrupt capi-
tal flight in anticipation of major devaluations. In 1969, interest rate
ceilings were raised for term deposits with banks, BCH certificates and
publicly-held government debt. In 1971, commercial banks were allowed to
issue Certificates of Term Deposits (CDTs) at interest rates substantially
higher than those paid on savings accounts.
3.03      Creation of the Housing Financing System in 1972.  The creation of
the CAVIs in 1972 was a major innovation in the Colombian financial market,
not only because it represented a further specialization within the system,
but also because CAVIs were allowed to index both their assets and
liabilities through the use of a "constant purchasing power unit" (UPAC)
linked to the consumer price index. Both lending and borrowing rates were
subject to ceilings, but these were expressed as margins, additional to the
monetary adjustment. Thus, the UPAC system represented a further
liberalization of the financial system to the extent that higher interest
rates (that were also closer to rates determined by market forces) were one
of its consequences. However, the introduction of the UPAC system had the
specific purpose of stimulating the housing and construction sector; in the
process, indexation was introduced. Since the actual returns on UPAC savings
accounts were considerably higher than the interest rates offered by
commercial banks, the rapid growth of UPAC deposits very soon constituted a
threat to the rest of the financial system which, by 1973, had began to lose
ground.



- 25 -
3.04      The Financial Reform of 1974-75.  In August 1974 the Government
began a financial reform; the approach was to deregulate the financial
sector, restoring its capacity to attract savings and to assign credit, as
well as reducing direct intervention by the Government. The ultimate
objectives were to improve productivity and to increase the saving rate. The
reform had six principal elements: (a) ceilings on deposit interest rates
were raised substantially--those on savings and term accounts were initially
raised from 8% to 12% annually, and CDTs from 12% to 24%; (b) DFC's were
allowed to issue short-term, high interest CDTs in the hope of increasing
working capital resources for industry; (c) interest rates on most loans were
freed; (d) the banking system's reserve requirements and forced investments
were reduced; (e) attempts to control the sectoral alLocation of credit were
substantially scaled down; and (f) the development banking role of the BR
was reduced: Instead, the BR was to concentrate on its role as monetary
authority, to which end it would make greater use of spen market operations.
3.05      These movements toward easing restrictions and eliminating
distortions were, however, partly offset by other measures. First, the
expansion of the UPAC system was limited by increased reserve requirements
and a 20% ceiling on the rate of indexation (which, in fact, implied the
elimination of indexation). Second, the 1974 Tax Reform had the side effect
of decreasing the after-tax return on savings, as personal income tax became
due on interest earned by savings accounts and returns on CATs with no
allowance for inflation. At the same time, withholding at the source was
instituted for interest earnings, thus reducing the scope for tax evasion.
Realized (nominal) capital gains were subjected to taxation at the same rates
as ordinary income, less an adjustment of only 8% for inflation, thereby
reducing the attraction of the stock market. Returns on UPAC deposits were
also treated in this way, further reducing their attractiveness. Thus,
changes in both the tax and UPAC systems partially offset the positive impact
of the financial reform on savings incentives.
3.06      The 1977 Stabilization Program.  The 1977 stabilization program
reversed some of the gains achieved in easing restrictions and eliminating
distortions in the financial system, particularly by restraining the growth
of liquidity (and inflation) through the repression of the financial sector
and by a policy of fiscal stringency. The program was a reaction to the
sharp increase in the rate of inflation that followed the foreign exchange
earnings boom due to the coffee bonanza and illegal exports. In addition,
initially, a slight slowdown in the pace of devaluation relative to local
inflation probably stimulated increased private foreign borrowing. The
Government viewed the incremental foreign exchange inflows as temporary and
eschewed adopting an import liberalization program as an adjustment measure.
Instead, the Government opted to limit imports and build up international
reserves in order to absorb liquidity.
3.07      The key features of the program were:  (a) initially, a 100%
marginal reserve requirement was instituted on the increase in demand
deposits, term deposits, and foreign currency deposits over their January
1977 levels; in mid-1977, this measure was replaced by an increase (three
percentage points) in the average reserve requirements on checking accounts,



- 26 -
plus a new (16.5%) secondary reserve requirements on the portfolio (excluding
funds raised via liabilities such as CDTs); (b) imposition of quantitative
restrictions on direct foreign borrowing and a 12 percentage points rise, to
18%, in the reserve requirements on foreign currency obligations of commer-
cial banks; (c) increased reserve requirements on UPAC deposits (to 15%) and
CDTs; (d) a substantial reduction in the money base, achieved by the
reimposition of prior deposits on imports. The increase in prior deposits
hact a contractionary monetary effect; however, reduction of imports tended
to decrease aggregate supply and contributed to the inflationay process; and
(e) a one-time delay in the monetization of international reserves, forcing
exporters of coffee and services to buy exchange certificates with their
foreign exchange receipts.
3.08       The system of directed credit was strengthened with a view to
protect certain productive sectors from the otherwise general credit tighten-
ing. This was done by allowing commercial banks and issuers of certificates
of deposit to avoid additional reserve requirements to the extent that they
made investments in low interest debt (8% to 18% depending on the type) of
the! BR development funds. These Funds were then used to rediscount loans
made to the priority sectors by commercial banks, the Caja Agraria, and
DFC 's.
3.09      As a result of these measures, the money multiplier fell sharply in
1977, offsetting the large primary expansion of the money base. This reduced
pressures on prices while allowing foreign exchange reserves to increase.
The switch to a tighter fiscal policy also had a contractionary effect but
gave the private sector access to funds formerly used by the Government (a
de-crowding-out effect). On the other hand, the cut-off in foreign borrowing
put upward pressure on interest rates. Similarly, the introduction of the
exchange certificate, the increase in prior deposits on imports and forced
investments (all of which had contractionary monetary effects) tended to
raise interest rates. On balance, the rise in forced investments roughly
offset the contractionary effects of prior deposits and exchange certificates
on the money base. Thus, the net result of this part of the program was not
a stabilizing reduction in monetary growth but simply an increase in directed
credit. In effect, a two-tiered credit market was created, which increased
the cost of non-directed credit.  Those with access to directed credit were
able to borrow at negative real interest rates while those who did not have
access faced a rise in real rates and, along with depositors, subsidized
directed credit. The growth of the unregulated financial market grew rapidly
using a number of ingenious devices to avoid the new reserve requirements.
By 1979 growing sophistication in avoiding restrictions had reduced credit
from the regulated financial market from 25% to 22% of GDP. Another conse-
quence was the intensification of the group movement, as industrial conglome-
rates sought to control access to cheap credit and banks sought to control
industries which benefitted from it.
3.10      Recent Developments, 1978-82.  Since 1978 the Government has made
graLdual adjustments in its financial sector policy, in light of changing
external and domestic economic developments, generally easing restrictions.
In 1978, the 100% marginal reserve requirement on foreign currency deposits
was replaced by an increased average rate requirement. The effect of
inflation on the personal income tax liability was partially recognized--tax



- 27 -
brackets and nominal capital gains were adjusted by increasing fractions of
the inflation rate until the Tax Alleviation Law of 1979 adopted 100% adjust-
ment. In January 1980, DFC's wTere allowed to issue CDTs with a free interest
rate and a 10% reserve requirement (to be invested in the Funds); banks are
now also allowed to enter this market. The unregulated market was brought
under tighter regulation, and the use of exchange certificates was reduced.
Reserve requirements on checking accounts were lowered in January 1980 and
again in September. In September 1980, interest rate ceilings on savings
accounts and UPACs were loosened slightly. Finally, since February 1982,
additional reductions were made in reserve requirements. The net effect of
the above measures has been to increase real returns to depositors and,
ultimately, resource mobilization. In addition, the measures have improved
the competitiveness of the regulated market relative to the unregulated one.
3.11      Prospects for Further Reforms.  The Government that took office in
the second half of 1982 is preparing a new financial reform. This reform is
expected to provide a more precisely defined framework for the new types of
financial institutions and operations that have proliferated in recent
years. Also, it would increase the supervisory powers of the monetary
authorities, in order to permit a more effective monitoring of the perform-
ance of financial institutions.
Liabilities of the Financial Sector
3.12      The liabilities of the major private financial intermediaries grew
quite rapidly from 1970 to 1981. The average growth rate was 33% per year,
well above the 28% average growth rate of money GDP during the same period.
The process of fInancial deepeni g of the Colombian economy reflected both
the increase in the community's demand for financial assets that accompanied
the improvement in living standards (per capita real GDP increased at an
average rate of 3% per year), as well as the increase in real interest rates
that was caused by the move towards financial liberalization up to 1977 and
the economic stabilization measures adopted since that year.
3.13      The process of financial deepening brought about a deep change in
the composition of the liabilities of the financial sector.  (Annex 3.2)
Demand deposits lost ground steadily, from nearly 60% of the sector's liabil-
ities in 1970 to less than 30% in 1981.  Two new instruments, that at the
beginning of the decade either did not exist (UPAC deposits) or were neglig-
ible (CDTs) jointly represented over 40% of the sector's liabilities by the
end of 1981.
3.14      Rapid growth and structural change have occurred in spite of the
fact that the public has not been offered a diversified menu as far as the
term structure of maturities is concerned. The overwhelming majority of the
liabilities offered to the public is short-term (up to one year). This
feature, of course, creates problems of term transformation for financial
institutions that seek to lend long or even mediumrterm and, as a result,
there is a concentration on short-term lending.



- 28 -
3.15      The tax treatment of interest earnings has hampered the creation of
medium and long-term financial liabilities. Interest earnings are taxed in
full under the highly progressive personal income tax instituted in 1974,
with no allowance for inflation. In an economy like Colombia's, where the
annual inflation rate can easily fluctuate between 20% and 25% and the
nominal interest rate on deposits can be a few percentage points greater,
what might appear to be a real interest rate of, say, 5% to 8% before tax,
can become a negative real rate after tax, even for savers that face moderate
marginal income tax rates. Further, interest earnings are subject to with-
holding at the source under the personal income tax, thus identifying the
earner and reducing the scope for tax evasion. Payments of "indexation" and
margins for deposits under the UPAC system are subject to the same tax treat-
ment except for the fact that they are allowed an arbitrary deduction of 8%
for inflation.
3.16      A consequence of this state of affairs is that CDTs--formally--earn
no interest. Rather, they are sold at a discount. Under the Colombian law
the difference between the face value of the document (received by the saver
at maturity) and the discounted value (at which the document was bought by
the saver) is a capital gain. More importantly, capital gains are not sub-
ject to withholding at the source which gives scope for tax evasion.
3.17      If tax considerations determine that financial liabilities must be
sold at a discount rather than pay interest, medium-term financial liabili-
ties would be seriously handicapped in the market place. Given the Colombian
pattern of sharply fluctuating interest rates in the short-run, a medium-term
discount paper would be subject to sharp price fluctuations in the short run,
that would reduce its liquidity.  For a given change in interest rates, the
magnitude of the corresponding price change would, of course, be magnified
for longer maturities. As a result, a medium-term paper would have to be
sold at overdiscounted prices to compensate for the reduced liquidity and
increased risk to the holder. It is not surprising then that the Colombian
financial market has failed to develop any significant medium-term
instruments.
3.18       For the Colombian financial market to offer good prospects for
medium-term bonds the interest rate would have to be variable, to accommodate
the interest rate fluctuations that often occurred in the past. In addition,
basic changes in the personal income tax treatment of interest earnings would
be required. As far as the project is concerned, the Government has agreed
to take measures to encourage medium and long-term investment in FEN paper
(paras. 2.14-2.16).
Assets of the Financial Sector
3.19      Total institutional credit outstanding as of the end of 1981 stood
at Col$525 billion. Its average annual growth rate from 1970 to 1981 was
31%, somewhat lower than the rate of growth of the financial sector's liabil-
ities, but higher than the average rate of growth of money incomes.
3.20      The relative importance of the sources of institutional credit has
changed over the last decade. (Annex 3.3) The specialized lending agencies
of the Government (BCH, Caja de Credito Agrario, Industrial, Minero, and Caja
Social de Ahorros) have lost ground as the importance of directed and subsi-
dized credit has declined. The share of commercial banks also fell somewhat



- 29 -
during the last few years.  CAVIs and CFCs, none of which existed at the
beginning of the decade, now represent nearly one-third of institutional
credit. As of the end of 1981, commercial banks still accounted for 40% of
the market, CAVIs 22%, financial corporations 17%, commercial finance comr-
panies 8% and the specialized lending agencies of the Government the remain-
ing 13%.
3.21      The distribution of institutional credit by term shows an increas-
ing share of short-term credit vis-a-vis medium- and long-term credit (Annex
3.4). Short-term credit accounted for 55% of total credit outstanding at the
end of 1981, while in 1970 it had represented only 41%. During the same
period the share of mediumrterm credit decreased from 25% to 17% and that of
long-term credit from 34% to 28%. The decline of long-term credit is more
marked if housing credit is excluded: from 17% in 1970 to only 10% in 1981.
FEN's Participation in Domestic Capital Markets
3.22      Because FEN will have to obtain an important part of its financial
resources in the domestic capital markets, the following paragraphs assess
the likely evolution of those markets--in light of Colombia's expected
economic performance--and the reasonable amounts of resources that FEN may be
expected to capture competitively without causing undesirable crowding-out
effects.
Recent Evolution of Financial Savings
3.23      From 1974 to 1981, the holdings of financial assets by the
publicl/ increased dramatically. By a factor of nearly ten in nominal
terms, or by a factor of 2.2 in real terms. As a ratio to GDP they grew from
18% in 1974 to 29% in 1981. The rapid growth of holdings of financial assets
must be attributed to the process of financial liberalization, that resulted
in higher average real interest rates offered to savers in a more competitive
environment, as well as to financial innovations introduced through the
Issuance of new short-term assets such as CDTs and UPAC deposits (Annex 3.5).
1/   The definition of financial assets held by the public used in this
section does not match the liabilities of the financial sector discussed
in paras. 3.12-3.18.  It includes holdings of Government bonds as well
as bonds issued by the Coffee Growers' Association. There is an element
of double counting to the extent that, for some assets gross holdings
had to be considered when net holdings could not be estimated.

FEN's Prospects
3.24       The process of financial deepening is expected to continue in
Colombia, though at a slower pace than that observed in the 1970s. Real GDP
is assumed to recover from the 1982 slowdown, with the growth rate reaching
2.5% in 1983 and 4% in 1984, and then rising less steeply reaching 5.6% by
1986. The inflation hypothesis is for a gradual decline, from 23%-24% in
1982-83 to 20% in 1986 and thereafter.
3.25       Projected holdings of financial assets would increase from 32.1% of
GDP in 1982 to 35.1% in 1987. The projections have been prepared on the
basis of estimates of the elasticy of (real) holdings of financial assets
with respect to (real) GDP. For this purpose, financial assets were classi-
fied onto "Ml" (currency and demand deposits), and other (interest earning)
financial assets. The elasticities used were 1.26 for the former and 1.37
for the latter.

3.27       Of course, all of these incremental resources will hardly be avail-
able to FEN, which will have to compete with other demanders of incremental
financial resources. By and large, the proportion of these funds that will
flow to FEN will depend on decisions made by the monetary authority. It is
conceivable that, initially, FEN might capture up to 50% of incremental
financial resources. But even if the initial proportion would be as high as
50%, reflecting the high priority that recent Governments haveattached to
investments in the power sector and the correspondingly high financial
requirements of ongoing projects, this proportion would in all likelihood
decline rapidly, maybe to 20%-25% (see below). If so, the financial
resources that FEN could obtain in the domestic capital markets would
increase from about Col$3-Col$8 billion in 1983 to Col$18 billion in 1987.
FEN's first experience in tapping the domestic market lends credence to the
estimates as Col$7 billion were raised by mid-1983 (para. 2.13).

4. THE PROJECT
Project Objectives
4.01       Of the two main project objectives, the first is to assist Colombia
in its efforts to provide the electricity supply required to support growth
and employment. At the present time, the country needs support to sustain,
during 1984-85, the scheduled construction pace of several high priority
electricity development projects in its scaled down investment program. De-
lays, mainly attributable to unforeseen geological characteristics and
insufficient financing availabilities, have already added 1 to 2 years to
estimated completion dates of various large hydroelectric facilities.
Further significant slippage is estimated to entail heavy electricity ration-
ing, despite the aforementioned slowing of demand. Several of these projects
have Bank loans associated with a portion of their foreign exchange cost, but
substantial additional external financing is required both to complete the
original financing plans and to cover cost increases. Although it had been
expected at the time these projects were appraised that the private financing
so readily available up to 24 months ago would continue to be obtainable as
needed, and on attractive terms, market conditions and experience today have
proven otherwise. For example, in 1980 private external lenders provided 55%
of the sector's foreign capital requirements and, although it had been anti-
cipated that this share would continue its upward trend and average about 75%
during 1981-90, this has not materialized. Therefore, the project also seeks
to re-enlist the support of foreign commercial lenders to the power sector,
through a US$170 million Bank A-loan together with a US$200 million B-loan
cofinancing package.
4.02       The second objective is to support the development of FEN.  As
Borrower for the Bank and B-loans, these would be the first external loans to
FEN. Since they would partially finance a time-slice of an investment prog-
ram, this operation represents a first sector lending project for power in
Colombia. It provides an opportunity to help ensure that, from an early
stage of FEN's operational life, the institution would have the capability to
be an effective channel for such lending and would foster reciprocal capabi-
lity in the power utilities. Ongoing efforts to strengthen the financial
management of the sector, as reflected in existing loan and other agreements,
would be buttressed further by the direct support to FEN to monitor the uti-
lities' financial performance and prospects, and act as power financing stra-
tegy advisor to the Government and the sector. Additionally, the loan would
further enhance FEN's image in the domestic capital market and, thus, help
facilitate its access to future savings.

going and future works, has been estimated at about Col$733 billion in
current prices (US$7.7 billion). Some 68% of these investments are for gene-

current prices, based upon reasonable cost estimates. The construction

The Project
4.07       The project consists of the 1984-1985 program of priority electri-
city development investments of ISA, EEEB, EPM and CVC (para. 4.06), with
contingent inclusion of ICEL and CORELCA, as well as a program to assist FEN
in strengthening its technical capability (para. 2.05). The time-slice is
subdivided into subprojects corresponding to that portion of electricity
development projects that would be underway during the period even though
completion of the works may extend beyond it. All subprojects under the pro-
ject are to be: (a) part of the National Power Expansion Program, in the
case of generation and transmission; (b) the least-cost solution for complet-
ing the network path to the final consumer, in the case of subtransmission
and distribution; or (c) additions to general plant needed to complement (a)
and (b) above. Additionally, for subprojects to be financed with the pro-
posed loan or the Bank's share of the cofinancing loans: (a) project costs
originally expected to be financed by commercial lenders would be eligible
but project cost overruns would not be, except for the Mesitas Hydro Power
Project (Annex 4.1); (b) interest during construction on existing Bank loans
would be eligible for financing in the cases of ISA, EEEB and EPM, all of

the total of the proposed loan and the Bank's proposed share (US$30 million)
of the B-loans (representing 15% of total Bank lending for the project). On
this basis together with the procurement norms described in the subprojects
eligible for financing under the proposed loan, and the Bank's share of the
cofinancing loans would include San Carlos I and II, Playas, Guadalupe IV,
and Guavio, as well as the Bogota Distribution Project. Subprojects eligible
for financing by commercial banks under the B-loans would include Chivor II

(partly financed by IDB), Termozipa V, Termocerrejon I and II, Betania,
Termotasajero, and other smaller installations, transmission lines and sub-

centers. Sub-loans from FEN to the utilities under the project would finance
foreign costs.   A list of priority subprojects that could be included in the

Subloans and Onlending Terms
4.09      Sub-loans financed under the proposed loan would require prior Bank
approval, except that sub-loans up to a total of US$4 million for a given
subproject would be free-limit sub-loans (other than the first three such
loans). Bank approval of free-limit sub-loans would, however, be required
prior to authorizing corresponding disbursements from the loan account. Each
sub-loan will be based upon detailed applications conforming to the afore-
mentioned criteria. FEN would approve applications for sub-loans under the
cofinancing B-loans only if the utility submitting the application would be
eligible to borrow under the proposed Bank loan; in particular, the utility
in question would need to remain current in payments to FEN under all loans
from the entity, and ICEL and CORELCA would need to fulfill certain condi-
tions precedent to their participation in any of the external financing to be
provided under the project (para. 4.12). Onlending terms of sub-loans will
be calculated upon the basis of full cost recovery by FEN on the Bank and
B-loans, and the foreign exchange risk will be borne by the utilities. Sub-
loans financed with the proceeds of the Bank and B-loans will be denominated
in dollars equivalent to the various currencies in which the principal of the
loans would be payable, and repayable in the peso equivalent of these various
currencies. Interest rate, fees and terms of the sub-loans are proposed to
be a blend of the terms of the Bank and cofinancing loans, plus a 0.5% spread
over the interest rate; maximum sub-loan term would be 15 years, including a
4-year grace period. The large volume of in-progress and future electricity
investments calls for the long-term maturities proposed. FEN will enter into
satisfactory sub-loan agreements with each sub-borrower.



- 37 -
Procurement and Disbursement Under the Bank Loan
4.10      Most contracts to be financed would have been let in advance of
Board presentation. As stated above in para. 4.06, retroactive financing,
from January 1, 1983, would be limited to US$29 million of the aggregate Bank
loan and its financial share of the cofinancing loan. Sub-loan proceeds will
be disbursed against contracts for goods and civil works which have been, or
are to be procured through international competitive bidding, in accordance
with Bank guidelines. The Bank's proposed share of the B-loans would be sub-
ject to the same procurement criteria as the Bank loan, and the commercial
lenders' share would follow procurement norms of the Bank, IDB, KfW or other
institutional lenders in the case of subprojects partly financed by the
entities, and local procedures of the Government--which are satisfactory to
the Bank--in the remaining cases.  The approximately 18 man-months of
consultant services to be engaged by FEN will be obtained according to Bank
guidelines; 100% of foreign expenditures, and 50% o-_ local, would be
financed.
4.11      Loan proceeds would be disbursed into a dollar-denominated revol-
ving fund in BR to be established by FEN, solely for the purposes of the pro-
ject. The Bank would make an initial deposit into the fund of US$50 million,
equivalent to estimated sub-loan disbursements during the first 90-day period
after loan effectiveness. Disbursements from the special account would be
made for eligible expenditures under each approved sub-loan. Special account
replenishment of the dollar equivalent amount of disbursements from that
account would be made upon receipt of withdrawal applications from FEN,
except that after disbursement of a total US$100 million, the Bank would
begin to recuperate the initial deposit. These applications would be under a
certified statement of expenditures by the sub-borrower concerned. Documen-
tation for these expenditures would be retained by FEN and available for
review by the Bank at all times. Disbursements are estimated at US$100
million in 1984 and US$70 million in 1985. In addition to annual external
auditing of FEN, the revolving fund account will be audited annually by
independent, external auditors acceptable to the Bank. The Closing Date of
the loan would be December 31, 1986.
Loan Structure
4.12      In addition to a draft Loan Agreement with FEN and a draft Guaran-
tee Agreement with the Republic, the loan documentation includes a draft
Power Financing Agreement between FEN and ISA, EEEB, EPM, CVC, CORELCA and
ICEL which establishes the general subloan terms and conditions. Further, in
this draft Agreement, the utilities reconfirm the relevant financial per-
formance covenants of the Guavio loan in respect of rate of return on annual-
ly revalued assets and internal cash generation. The Agreement also provides
for a capital expenditure limitation, and debt service coverage.  In the case
of IGEL, for which it has not been possible to establish financial targets or
even a reasonable financial forecast, and in the case of CORELCA, they would
be eligible to receive proceeds from the proposed Bank and cofinancing
B-loans only after preparation by November 30, 1984, of Government-endorsed
investment and financing programs, satisfactory to the Bank. For each com-
pany, these programs should cover the years 1984-87, including the additional



- 38 -
years in which the proposed La Miel (ICEL) and Urra (CORELCA) power genera-
tion projects would be carried out. The programs would be accompanied by a
Government commitment to take all actions necessary, including timely provi-
sion of funds, to enable ICEL and CORELCA to carry out the programs, as well
as to service any sub-loans which they may receive from the proposed external
loans. In the event that plans satisfactory to the Government and the Bank
would not materialize by the date mentioned above, the company involved would
become ineligible to borrow under the loans, and the approximately US$40
million of total Bank and cofinancing loan proceeds that would be held in
abeyance for them until that time would be committed to priority ongoing pro-
jects of other companies. However, the sector financial analysis (Chapter 5)
assumes that total external loan proceeds would be allocated to only the four
major companies. In addition, the Government would: (a) by December 31,
1984, establish in ICEL adequately staffed Financial and Operations Depart-
ments; and (b) cause ICEL to carry out, by December 31, 1984 a study to
determine the value of its assets. Additionally, ISA would participate in
the annual review of the power sector's investment program and financing
strategy, in a financial study to be carried out by FEN, and in the design of
an appropriate construction price index for the sector. Any failure by the
parties to the Power Financing Agreement would constitute an event of suspen-
sion under the Loan Agreement.
Benefits and Risks
4.13      The proposed project will assist Colombia to maintain construction
rhythm of ongoing electricity development projects which will supply the
electric energy needed to facilitate growth of those industrial and commer-
cial activities which currently use it, as well as help supply electricity to
a larger segment of the country's population, only 54% of which now have
access to it. The participation of the international capital market is being
enlisted in this endeavor. The support provided for the development of FEN
is expected to enhance power sector financial management, in addition to the
buttressing the entity's ability to mobilize substantial investment financing
needed by the power utilities.
4.14      A return on investment for the project has not been quantified.
IHowever, it has been established that the projects which are prospective sub-
loan candidates for Bank financing would have higher internal rates of return
than the 13%-15% estimated at the time of original Bank appraisal. Tariffs
(the proxy for benefits) have increased more rapidly than projected, while
average costs are approximately the same. Further, for all hydroelectric
facilities included under the project, representing about 80% of project
costs, the average cost remains a low US$700 per kW installed. Subtrans-
mission and distribution facilities would represent the least-cost means of
bringing electricity service to the final consumer. There is a risk that the
local currency share of investments may not be available in a timely manner.
However, the expected internal cash generation of the four major utilities,
the measures already taken by the Colombian authorities to mobilize savings,
as well as future actions that have been agreed should enable project financ-
ing to materialize as scheduled.  There is another risk, which is attendant
mainly upon FEN's ability to fulfill its role as a development banking insti-
tution. By virtue of its financial significance, there may be pressures
brought to bear upon its decisions. However, the establishment of sound
lending operating criteria, the support provided for its monitoring and



- 39 -
advisory role and the broad-based composition of its Board and Credit
Committee--as spelled out in the entity's Bylaws--together with the
agreements reached with the Government and the power utilities on the condi-
tions under which new projects would be initiated, should help minimize this
risk.
5. POWER SECTOR FINANCES
Introduction
5.01      This section deals with the financial performance and outlook of
the main electric companies which are shareholders of FEN. As pointed out in
Chapter 4, ISA, EEEB, EPM and CVC will be the beneficiaries of the proposed A
and B loans; CORELCA and ICEL will be eligible for subloans provided that key
conditions will be met beforehand (para. 5.13).  Set out below is the present
and projected financial situation of each utility, followed by a consolidated
analysis of the sector.  This has been done under the coordination of ISA
with Bank assistance and is based on an agreed methodology and assumptions.
The 1983-87 projections are based on the National Power Expansion Program
adopted in 1983 (para. 1.20).
5.02      ISA generates, operates the interconnected system and sells in
bulk to its five shareholders, who are connected to the national grid and
also generate power in their own plants as well as take power from the grid.
ISA's shareholders contribute capital for the financing of all major projects
connected to the grid, which are constructed either by ISA or by its share-
holders with ISA participation in the investment; thus the finances of the
individual companies cannot be fully analyzed in isolation. There are sub-
stantial fund flows between the companies and financial problems in one com-
pany may affect others. This creates the need for continuous and systematic
monitoring of an individual company and sectoral financial performance as
well as careful attention to tariff policy at the national level (para.
5.14).
5.03      ISA
(a)  1977-82 Financial Performance has been satisfactory based mainly on
balanced operating results and the equity contributed by its share-
holders. Rates of return were in the range of those covenanted
with Bank as well as other financial indicators forecast in the San
Carlos and Guavio loans (Annexes 5.1 and 5.2). Since 1980 increa-
sing arrears from shareholders (mainly ICEL, EEEB and ultimately
CVC) in energy bills and capital contributions have adversely
affected ISA's ability to make timely payments to contractors,
causing delays in projects under construction.
(b)  Investment and Financing Plan:  ISA's 1984-85 and 1984-87
investment program and financing plan is summarized below and shown
in detail in Annex 5.5.

(c)  Outlook:  ISA's financial projections (Annexes 5.1.4 to 5.1.6) seem to
be acceptable. In principle, financial equilibrium should be easy for
ISA to maintain. It has a captive market with only 5 large wholesale
customers (its shareholders), long-term sale contracts and good
mechanisms for increasing tariffs when needed based on agreed financial
targets. In addition, ISA has firm commitments from its shareholders
(based on its by-laws) and the Government for capital contributions to
finance the local costs of its construction program and debt service.
Finally, ISA's present financial situation is strong, having external
loans from the Bank, IDB, suppliers and commercial banks, which cover
the foreign currency requirements for much of its ongoing construction
program; the remaining balance needed for 1984-85 is to be funded by the
proposed A and B loans.   Consequently, ISA's main financial risk is the
ability of its shareholders or the Government to fulfill their financial
commitments to it.



- 41 -
5.04       EEEB
(a)  Financial Performance: Over its 23-year relationship with the Bank
EEEB has maintained a sound financial position, with a slightly
declining trend since 1979.  Internal cash generation provided
about 50% of its investments on average, including its contribu-
tions to ISA. EEEB enjoys a high-density market and the area's
geographical advantages for hydro generation. The balance of the
company's investment funds has been obtained through borrowings; no
Government funding or other capital contributions have been
involved. Between 1979 and 1981, rate of return and self-financing
ratios were slightly below those covenanted in Bank loans (Annexes
5.7 and 5.8); however, debt/equity ratio and debt service coverage
remained at comfortable levels. Nevertheless, EEEB's arrears in
payments to ISA and to its own contractors increased sharply and
with consequent delays in construction projects (Mesitas, Bogota
Distribution and Guavio). This resulted from (i) tariff increases,
even through reasonable in nominal terms, were eroded by a higher
than expected rate of increase in operating costs (para. 5.15);
(ii) electricity rationing: lower than projected energy sales and
relatively higher energy purchases from ISA which is more costly
than EEEB's own generation; (iii) a heavy investment program:
existing total fixed assets have doubled in real terms in the
3-year period 1980-82; (iv) insufficient local financing and (v)
absence of compensating capital contributions from Government or
other sources.
(b)  Investment and Financing Plan:  EEEB's 1984-85 and 1984-87 invest-
ment program and financing plan is summarized below and shown in
detail in Annex 5.9.

(c)  Outlook:  Notwithstanding the trend described in (a) above, EEEB's
financial outlook appears adequate provided the basic projection
assumptions regarding tariff levels and sufficient financing are
met. Tariffs are projected to increase at a faster rate than
operating costs (Annexes 5.10 to 5.12).  This comprises the present
tariff increase program already authorized by JNT (Resolution No.
05/82) and comprises a one-time, flat 21% increase enacted in
October 1982 and, from November 1982 to December 1984, a monthly
increase averaging 3.25%l/; for 1985 and thereafter a 20% annual
increase was assumed.  This is estimated to meet the rate of return
and self-financing targets agreed with the Bank. The remaining
financing needed for ongoing investments during 1984-85 would be
provided under the project.
5.05      EPM
(a)  Financial Performance:  EPM's Power Department (as well as the
Telephone, Water and Sewerage Department) has had a history of
adequate operating results and satisfactory overall financial per-
formance (Annexes 5.13 and 5.14). Self-financing, averaging 60%
for the 1977-82 period, has adequately complemented its investments
requirements including recent contributions to ISA; this may be
attributed in large part to the high density of its market and very
low-cost facilities, given the area's geographic advantages for
hydro generation. The balance for the company's investment
requirements has been obtained through borrowings. No Government
funding or capital contributions have been involved.
(b)  Investment and Financing Plan:  EPM's 1984-85 and 1984-87
investment program and financing plan is summarized below and shown
in detail in Annex 5.15.

in line with adjusted inflation assumptions for 1984.

(c)  Outlook:  It shows a viable picture provided the achievement of
forecast assumptions and targets, especially the balance between
tariffs and operating costs and availability of domestic financing
(Annexes 5.16 to 5.18). EPM's present financial situation is
sound; the company is the strongest of ISA's shareholders. The
present tariff increase program approved by EPM's Board and author-
ized by JNT is comprised of a monthly increase of 2.2% with no
terminal date specified for such monthly increase. Financial
forecast is based on (a) continuation of the 2.2% monthly increase
through 1984; (b) a 15% punctual increase in June 1984; and
(c) 1.8% monthly increase during 1985-1987. It is expected that
this program of tariff increases, which is in line with current
Government policy, will be accepted by JNT before the presentation
to the Board of the proposed loan for EPM's Rio Grande Project,
currently scheduled for July 1984. Proceeds of the Bank and
cofinancing loans will complete the financing requirements for the
1984-85 program of ongoing construction.

5.06      CVC
(a)  Financial Performance:  Since 1977 CVC has had an acceptable
financial performance, however, some difficulties have recently
arisen. Its financial performance has been characterized by two
main factors: (a) tariff increases that slightly surpassed the
cost of service; and (b) a relatively modest investment program, of
which 50% has been contributions to ISA. Rates of return were in
the range covenanted in the San Carlos Shareholders Agreement, and
self-financing averaged 68% in 1978-82 period; capital contribur-
tions by consumers - rural electrification - were about 2% and the
remaining balance was funded by borrowings (Annexes 5.19 and
5.20). Starting in late 1981, insufficient financing relative to
the requirements of its on-going contruction program (mainly the
Salvajina hydro project) has strained CVC's financial situation
resulting in significant arrears to ISA. Since 1977 no Government
funding or capital contributions have been involved.
(b) Investment and Financing Plan: CVC's 1984-85 and 1984-87 invest-
ment program and financing plan is summarized below and shown in
detail in Annex 5.21.

(c)  Outlook:  Despite the developments described in (a) above the pre-
sent financial position and forecast of CVC appear to be adequate
(Annexes 5.22 to 5.24), although highly tied to the availability of
timely financing and to a sustained tariff increase aimed at pro-
viding the required self-financing. The present tariff increase
program authorized by JNT on which the financial forecast was
based, comprises a 2.2% monthly increase for 1984 and 1985. Annual
increases equivalent to expected inflation have been assumed for
1985 and thereafter. Proposed financing from the proceeds of the
Bank and cofinancing loans will fund the unfinanced balance of
ongoing construction, except for a small balance of US$5 million
that is to be covered by planned Government borrowings in 1985.
5.07      CORELCA Group
The CORELCA Group consist of a holding company CORELCA, which is
responsible for power generation and transmission, and eight distributing
subsidiary companies ("electrificadoras"), which mainly purchase power in
bulk from CORELCA for retail sales in the eight departments of the Atlantic
Region; the electrificadoras generate only minor amounts of energy. The
historic financial analysis referred to below is based on CORELCA's perform-
ance because historic statements of the consolidated group are not complete;
however, the financial outlook for the 1983-85 period is based on the conso-
lidated statements of CORELCA and its eight electrificadoras.
(a)  Financial Performance:  CORELCA's 1977-82 financial performance has
been poor, with a deteriorating trend. Rates of return ranged
below the targets of the San Carlos II and Village Electrification
Loans as well as the other financial indicators (Annexes 5.25 and
5.26). The main reasons for this picture are: (i) lower sales
growth than expected; (ii) low revenues level in comparison with
its operating costs (operating ratio averages 80% in 1980-82);
(iii) heavy debt service resulting in a coverage averaging 0.7 for
1980-82; and (iv) high accounts receivable for the energy sold
mainly from Government and municipal agencies.
(b)  Investment and Financing Plan:  CORELCA's 1984-1985 and 1984-87
investment program and financing plan is summarized below and shown
in detail in Annex 5.27.

(c)  Outlook:  The outlook of the CORELCA Group is uncertain, mainly
because of the lack of a financing plan for the local component of
the Urra hydro power and Termocartagena projects (Annexes 5.28 to
5.29). The construction of Urra has been allocated to CORELCA by
ISA, with CORELCA having a financial participation of 44% of local
costs. Assuming that the foreign component of the Urra and
Termocartagena projects (the latter has not been authorized by
CONPES) are financed by external loans, the local financing
envisaged by CORELCA (ISA's share of local costs of Urra and
CORELCA's net internal generation of funds)is not sufficient to
fund the local investment requirements of the project, resulting in
significant deficits in the 1984-87 period (Annex 5.29); the
financing of such gaps, would exceed the borrowing capability of
CORELCA. The Government together with ISA and CORELCA are
currentiy studying alternative investment and financing options,
which will bear directly upon CORELCA's eligibility to borrow under
the proposed Bank and cofinancing loans (para. 5.13).
5.08       ICEL Group
(a)  Financial Performance:  The ICEL Group (a holding company and its
13 departmental subsidiaries) has a history of poor financial per-
formance in terms of banking standards because of the characteris-
tics of its market and the influence of political considerations in
its administration. Adequate and reliable financial statements of
the ICEL Group for the period 1977-82 are not available. However,
some basic financial indicators for the 1979-82 period reflect the
precariousness of its finances: averages of operating revenues and
operating costs (computing non-revalued depreciation) per kWh sold
virtually equivalent; debt service coverage less than one; and net
internal cash generation consistently negative. Debt service and
construction programs have been partially funded with contributions
from Government, which also provides ICEL's capital contributions
to ISA, although usually with substantial delay. ICEL has a
geographically dispersed market (about 75% of the Colombian terri-
tory comprising approximately 56% of its population), that is pre-
dominantly rural, including low and middlelow income segments of
population with few large cities or significant industrial areas.



- 47 -
These features result in high operating costs.   Furthermore, the
Group has not been efficiently managed: (a) the boards and manage-
ment of subsidiaries tend to be highly politicized; (b) there is
often strong opposition to tariff increases at the local level; (c)
investments may be undertaken without consideration of economic
feasibility; and (d) there is resistance to redistribution of
markets and regrouping subsidiaries. The Government had realized
that the ICEL Group needs be restructured to become financially
viable and operationally efficient; consequently, in November 1980
CONPES, based upon recommendations of DNP, approved a number of
measures aimed at attaining those efficiency targets. Sub-
sequently, during the Guavio loan negotiations, the Government
agreed to prepare a program for implementing DNP's recommendation
regarding the structure and operations of ICEL, to be put into
effect starting June 30, 1983. Although some measures have been
taken the reorganization will necessarily be achieved only
gradually, over a period of years.
(b)  Investment and Financing Plan:  ICEL's 1984-85 and 1984-1987
investment program and financing plan is summarized below and shown
in detail in Annex 5.31.

(c)  Outlook:  The outlook of the ICEL Group is unpredictable based upon
the financial picture described above and the lack of a financing
plan for its large investment program in which the La Miel hydro
project is the main component (Annexes 5.32 and 5.33). Operating
results and internal cash generation forecast for the 1982-87
period are negative. The construction of La Miel (granted to ICEL
and CHEC - a subsidiary - by ISA) as well as other projects of the
investment program would require financing exceeding the borrowing
capability of ICEL. At present, the Government has under study
alternative investment and financing schemes for ICEL which,
together with other measures, will determine ICEL's eligibility as
a subborrower under the Bank and cofinancing loans (paras. 4.12 and
5.13)
5.09       Betania
(a)   Background:  Betania is a new autonomous company, a subsidiary of
ICEL, which has been been created for the construction and
operation of the Betania hydro plant (para. 1.18) which is
currently forecast to be commissioned in 1987.

Financial Outlook of the Sector
5.10       The financial picture of the sector resulting from the consolidated
financial projections of the six utilities analyzed above is detailed in
Annexes 5.36 and 5.37.
5.11       Investment and Financing Plan.  The power sector's 1984-87 invest-
ment program, including construction, interest during construction, out of
the sector investments and requirements for working capital amounts to
MCol$771,400 (MUS$7,619), of which 31% corresponds to ongoing works and 47%
to future works, The sector's net internal cash generation would finance
MCol$143,000 (MUS$1,421) or 19% of the total; capital contributions from the
national budget and consumers for various distribution programs woald fund
10%; borrowings 61%, of which 12 percentage points corresponds to existing
loans, 4 percentage points to the proposed IBRD-FEN loan and 45 percentage
points to future loans.  The remaining MCol$79,500 (MtS$785), or 10%, is the
estimated financing gap(forecast by CORELCA and ICEL), which funding is
presently under consideration by the Government. The investment program and
financing plan is summarized below.

- 50 -
5.12      The scenario above shows an overall imbalance resulting from the
unwieldy deficits forecast by CORELCA and ICEL, in contrast with an adequate
picture of the four remaining companies, which may be affected if the
responsibility for construction of the Urra and La Miel projects is changed.
Existing agreements with the companies concerned provide that they would not
incur capital expenditures to increase their individual generating capacity
in excess of 100 MW unless the proposed investment is economically justified
as part of the Least-Cost National Power Expansion Program and that there
would be financing available which would not affect negatively the respective
company's existing financial obligations. This principle has been
re-confirmed during negotiations of the proposed A-loan: the Government has
undertaken to authorize the initiation of new electricity development
projects only in the event that such projects are economically justified and
that the respective power company disposes adequate financing (paras. 1.26
and! 4.12). Furthermore, each of the power companies has agreed not to commit
itself to any capital expenditure until the on-going works have been
completed, unless the proposed expenditure: (a) is economically justified;
and (b) does not exceed in the aggregate the equivalent of one percent (1%)
of the current net value of its current assets in operation. However, the
power companies may incur in such expenditures in excess of such one percent
(1%) if they have obtained financing which will not adversely affect their
financial performance.
5.13      Despite the uncertainties involved in the financial outlook for the
sector described above, it would be counterproductive to delay this proposed
operation until they are resolved. One of the strong features of ISA, EEEB,
EPM and CVC is their low debt/equity ratios (Annexes 5.2, 5.8, 5.14 and
5.20). Therefore, if they meet their performance targets - sales, tariffs
and operating expenses - they would be able to increase their indebtedness to
the levels in their forecasts without jeopardizing their financial
situations. ICEL and CORELCA, because of the precariousness of their
finances, are not yet sound credit risks; their access to loan proceeds will
be contingent upon the actions and agreements cited in para. 4.12.
5.14      The picture resulting from the financial consolidation points to
the need for periodic work on defining and updating the financing strategy
for the electric sector. During negotiations of the proposed loan, agreement
was reached that an annual review of such strategy will take place with the
Government, FEN and ISA, with the first one to be held in September 1984 (see
1.20 and below). Even though a more favorable scenario has resulted from the
recent revision of the Expansion Program by postponements of ongoing
projects, and by possible changes in the ownership/participation arrangements
for future projects, a gap to be financed remains irrespective of the new
scenario. Taking into consideration the magnitude of the investment programs
(the present or any likely alternative) and DNP's estimate of the capital
contributions from the Government, the area of tariffs and revenue efficiency
needs further analysis.  Financing mechanisms such as funds or surcharges to
transfer capital to, or within, the electric sector do not seem to be
feasible in Colombia because of regional considerations and because each
utility's investment program would have a balance to be financed. To depend
excessively on borrowings would jeopardize the financial health of the sector
in the medium term. All of these factors need to be analyzed regularly in
the light of planned capital outlays. FEN has decided to carry out a study



- 51 -
on sector finances and formulate a proposed financing strategy based upon the
approved 1987-92 Expansion Program, which will form the basis for the first
annual review. This proposal would define a balanced mix of the different
sources of financing (internal cash generation and its linkage with the
tariff policy, capitalization from the national budget or other sources, and
local and external borrowings policies) for the Expansion Program, aimed at
promoting a feasible medium-term plan. During loan negotiations, agreement
was reached that FEN will complete the study by September 15, 1984 and it
will be discussed with the Government, ISA and the Bank. ISA will
coordinate with FEN in the preparation of the study and, together with its
shareholders, give all necessary assistance to FEN. This exercise will be
repeated annually by FEN.
5.15      Evidence, suggests that the Colombian consumer price index, which
is used for asset revaluation and costing purposes, underestimates power
sector costs. Since 1977, Bank loans to the power sector have provided for
annual asset revaluation based upon changes in the consumer price index (blue
collar) as a proxy for the replacement value of assets in operation and con-
struction. This index has also been used to project operating and local
investment costs. However, ISA has demonstrated that an index based on the
construction of civil works of the San Carlos hydro project, in the period
1977-82, is 7% per annum higher than the consumer price index, and 32% accu-
mulated for the period. Use of an unsuitable index has significant negative
effects on the financial administration of the companies because: (a)
investment requirements as well as the projected cost of service is
underestimated; (b) financial indicators are misleading, e.g., rates of
return are overstated. During negotiations agreement was reached that the
Government will prepare appropriate price indexes for use by the electric
utilities. The Government, through its statistical agency, DANE, with
assistance from ISA would design such an index, and by December 31, 1984,
present it to the Bank for review and comment. An agreed index will be
computed and published quarterly, starting in January 1985.



- 52 -
6. AGREEMENTS REACHED AND RECOMMENDATION
6.01    During negotiations of the proposed loan, agreement has been reached:
(a)  that the Government would authorize capital outlays for new electri-
city projects only if the project in question would be economically
justified and there would be available adequate financing that would
not affect negatively the existing financial obligations of the
company(-ies) concerned (para. 1.26);
(b)  that any change in FEN statutory framework, credit policy and
regulations, or access to short-term borrowing from BR that, in the
Bank's opinion would have an adverse effect, would constitute events
of default under the proposed loan (paras. 2.03, 2.29 and 2.31);
(c)  that by June 30, 1984, FEN will engage consultants to complement its
technical capability in the areas of project analysis and monitoring
(para. 2.05);
(d)  that FEN will engage the services of independent auditors acceptable
to the Bank and maintain internal accounting and auditing systems in
accordance with sound principles (para. 2.06);
(e)  that FEN will annually capitalize its profits (para. 2.07);
(f)  that, by December 31, 1984, FEN would prepare a plan of action
acceptable to the Government and the Bank to encourage private
investment in medium and long-term instruments of FEN and, promptly
thereafter, the Government would put such plan into effect (para.
2.16);
(g)  on annual targets for mobilization of domestic financial savings by
FEN, which targets would be reviewed and updated, as necessary, by
agreement among FE-N, the Government and the Bank (para. 2.17);
(h)  with the Government, that all necessary measures on its part would be
taken to provide FEiJ with adequate access to the domestic capital
market (para. 2.17);
(i)  that FEN will manage its finances in such a manner as to meet the
following annual targets: FEN will maintain at all times a liquidity
buffer equivalent to not less than two months of the total of: esti-
mated expenditures plus half of the principal amount that would
become due and payable to local investors during the year; these
estimates would be agreed during the annual review discussed below.
Additionally, FEN would maintain a debt-equity ratio not to exceed
7:1. Agreement was also reached that FEN, the Government and the
Bank will review annually, beginning in March 1984, the entity's
financial performance and prospects. Further, FEN will not authorize
new lending to any borrower which is in default under any loan
previously authorized by FEN (para. 2.31);



- 53 -
(j)  on the criteria for sub-project selection, financing, procurement and
disbursement procedures (paras. 4.08-4.11);
(k)  that FEN's revolving fund accounts in BR will be audited annually by
independent, external auditors acceptable to the Bank (para. 4.11);
(1)  that ISA, EEEB, EPM, CVC and CORELCA accept the general onlending
terms and conditions. Confirmation of the financial performance
agreements reached under the most recent power loan has been obtained
from each utility, including rates of return on annually revalued
assets, internal cash generation. Agreement has also been reached on
capital expenditure limitations and debt-service ratio. Further, the
Government will ensure that adequately staffed Financial and
Operations Departments are established in ICEL. A study will be
carried out to determine the value of ICEL's fixed assets. Addition-
ally, ISA will participate in annual Power Expansion Program and
financial strategy reviews, the sector financial study to be carried
out by FEN and in the design of an appropriate price index for the
sector. These assurances would be covenanted in a Power Financing
Agreement between FEN and the power companies; a default by any of
the parties to this Agreement would constitute an event of default
under the Loan Agreement. Conditions governing ICEL and CORELCA's
eligibility to sub-loans have also been agreed (para. 4.12);
(m)  on the proposed revolving fund and on the related documentation pro-
cedures (para. 4.15);
(n)  that the Government, FEN and ISA, together with the Bank, would
review annually the financing strategy for the power sector. The
first such review would take place in September 1984, based upon a
study of sector finances to be carried out by FEN. (paras. 5.13 and
6.01(j)). This study would take account of updated electricity
demand growth projections and the associated Expansion Program, which
would be prepared by ISA and reviewed by the Government by April 30
of each year, beginning in 1984 (para. 1.20); and
(o)  that the Government, with the participation of ISA, would prepare an
appropriate price index for the electricity sector and, by December
31, 1984, present it to the Bank for review and comment. An agreed
index would be computed and published quarterly by the Government,
starting in January 1985 (para. 5.14).
6.02    The assignment of a short-term borrowing quota to FEN by the BR would
be a condition of effectiveness of the proposed loan (para. 2.12). Prior to
the first sub-loan, FEN will present a satisfactory sub-loan agreement to the
Bank (para. 4.09).
6.03    Based upon the agreements outlined above, the proposed project would
constitute a suitable basis for a Bank loan of US$170 million with a term of
17 years including 4 years of grace.

COLOIBIA
FEN POWER DEVELOPMENT FINANCE
Mediumr and Long-Term Financing in Countries with High Inflation
1.        Excluding public and external sources, the essential problem which
the FEN faces is that of providing medium-and long-term credit to the
electric sector, with funds raised in a market that is almost exlusively
short-term. This is also the case with the other Colombian development
finance institutions (corporaciones financieras). In fact, it is a problem
endemic to development finance institutions in countries with relatively high
inflation.
2.        This Annex demonstrates how alternative medium- and long-term
financing systems can contribute to coping with such a situation, and focuses
on the conditions required to generate such funding.
Evaluation of Alternative Medium- and
Long-Term Credit Systems for Colombia l/
3.        There are four main types of credit instruments:
(a) Fixed Interest Rate. Under inflationary conditions, price movement
expectations are high; thus lenders and borrowers do not find a
mutually-acceptable long-term interest rate;
(b) Fixed Interest Rate and Indexed Principal. Experience shows that,
on these terms, households tend to be willing, but corporations
reluctant to borrow on these terms; additionally, investors are
unwilling to lend;
(c) Floating Interest Rate. Under persistently high inflation, a large
part of the corresponding high nominal interest rate represents
repayment of real principal, which reduces the real/mean maturity
of the credit and usually imposes a heavy burden on the cash flow
of the borrower; and
(d) Floating Interest Rate and (partially) Capitalized Interest. There
has been almost no experience with such instruments.
4.        The adequacy of mediumr-and long-term credit systems can be
evaluated in terms of their contribution to a financial system's objective
of:2/
A. extending maturities;
/   The next paragraphs are based upon Veneroso, Frank J. "Colombia,
Interest Rate Policy Issues," July 1982.
2/   The objectives enumerated here correspond to those in the table in
paragraph 6.



- 60 -             Annex 2.1
Page 2 of 4
B. clearing the market through a saleable interest rate;
C. stabilizing the market; and
D. stabilizing prices.
5.        With these objectives in mind, the most promising variations of the
four main medium- and long-term credit instruments mentioned above can be
evaluated:
I.   Asset:       Long-term; fixed interest rate and indexed principal.
Liability:  Short-term deposit; fixed interest rate and indexed
principal.
II.  Asset:       Long-term; variable interest rate and indexed
principal.
Liability:  Short-term deposit; variable interest rate and indexed
principal.
III.  Asset:       Long-term; floating interest rate and (partially)
capitalized interest.
Liability:  Short-term, fixed interest rate.
IV.  Asset:       Long-term; fixed interest rate and indexed principal.
Liability:  Long-term bond; fixed interest rate and indexed
principal.
V.  Asset:       Long-term, floating interest rate and (partially)
capitalized interest.
Liability:  Long-term bond; floating interest rate and (partially)
capitalized interest.
6.        The table set out below summarizes the positive (+) or negative ()
contributions 3/ of each of these five variations to the stated
objectives.  Assignment of (+) or (-) is based upon the following
considerations:
(a) all variations contribute to extend maturities because they have
been so selected;
(b) only variable interest rate instruments of fixed interest rate
papers with flexible values produce the right interest rate. This
is also true for the stability of financial markets; and
3/   The table does not capture the intensity of effects; only their
direction.



- 61 -              Annex 2.1
Page 3 of 4
(c)  only variable interest rate instruments "accompany" monetary
policy; indexed instruments project past inflation into the future.
Variations                       Objectives
A     B      C     D 4/
I                      +      _     _      _
II                      +      -     -      -
III                      +      +     +      +
IV                      +      +     +
V                      +      +     +      +
7.        According to this analysis, the better variations are V and III,5/
followed by IV.  In brief, the best systems have long-term assets with
floating interest rates and (partially) capitalized interest funded by either
short-term, fixed interest rate liabilities or by long-term bonds with
floating interest rates and (partially) capitalized interest. The next
alternative is long-term assets with fixed interest rate and indexed
principal financed by bonds of similar characteristics.
The Generation of Resources for
Medium- and Long-Term Lending b/
8.        The avenues for financing medium- and long-term lending are:
extending the term transformation of short-term resources and lengthening the
maturity of funding.
9.         Term Transformation.  Term transformation creates two main problems
for financial institutions, an interest rate and a liquidity risk. Interest
rate risk (the difference between lending and borrowing rates) can be
controlled by tying the floating interest rate of long-term assets to the
cost of short-term funds. This linkage reduces the interest rate risk to
variations in the institution's and the market's borrowing rates, and also
helps to reduce the liquidity risk. The only remaining rate risk is that of
official regulation of, or influence upon the interest rates of the
short-term instruments used for funding. Liquidity risk are reduced, first
and foremost, by the existence of a liberal financial syster--which tends to
reduce large transfers of funds between instruments--and central bank
rediscount facilities to cushion temporary drops in the level of funds raised
by the institutions.
4/   See paragraph 4.
5J   The reason for this ordering is explained in the following section.
6/   The following paragraphs are based upon Silva Lopes, J. "Colombia,
Resources for Mediumr and Long-Term Lending by The Corporaciones
Financieras," August 10, 1982.



- 62 -              Annex 2.1
Page 4 of 4
10.       Maturity of Resources.  In the previous section, the best
instruments to lengthen the maturity of resources are identified as medium-
and long-term bonds with a floating interest rate and (partially) capitalized
interest.7/ The design of such instrument can be sketched as follows:
(a)  Floating Interest Rate:  to be set at the beginning of each
quarter, by the last weekly 90-day interest rate index, plus a
constant spread, with maturity premia;
(b)  Risk of Capital Losses:  if official intervention is likely to
affect the index used to determine the floating interest rate, the
criteria for shifting to an alternative control-free index should
be predetermined;
(c)  Liquidity Risk:  bonds should be quoted in the stock exchange to
provide secondary liquidity. There should be neither a
stabilization (support) fund nor a guarantee of repurchase at par.
There should, however, be a limited liquidity fund, with the sole
function of providing a buyer or a seller if needed;8/
(d)  Maturity and Amortization:  three to five years (i.e., a 3-year
bond with repayment of 1/3 of its nominal value each year);
(e)  (Partially) Interest Capitalized:  the market should choose among
the three alternative methods:
(i) the rate of interest to be paid is set in advance and the rest
is capitalized. For example, the floating rate for the four
quarters of the year is 28.3%; if the rate of interest to be
paid is set at 8%, Lihen at the end of the year the institution
pays 8% and the remaining 21.3% is added to the principal, to
be paid at maturity);
(ii) the percentage to be amortized is predetermined and
capitalized; the rest is paid out as interest; and
(iii) the share of the total interest to be paid is pre-set, and the
rest capitalized.
11.       The first method is, in fact, preferable because it maintains the
amount of interest payment constant, thus facilitating financial planning and
cutting operational costs.
12.       Taxation.  Only real interest should be taxed; since real interest
is only known ex-post, it can be pegged ex ante by setting a de jure
anticipated monetary correction.
7/   The alternative of long-term bonds with fixed interest risk and indexed
principal follows far behind.
8/   Note that long-term bonds with fixed interest rate and indexed principal
require large stabilization funds.

COLOMBIA
FEN POWER DEVELOPMENT FINANCE
Basis for FEN's Financial Projections
Liabilities and Sources
1.        Projected liabilities and sources are based on the following
assumptions:
(a) Capital Resources. FEN's shareholders complete in 1983 the paying
in of their capital subscriptions. The premium FEN's new share-
holders pay as late subscribers is included in profits. Any
further increase of capital resources comes from undistributed pro-
fits. Annual profits amount to about 5% of the sum of initial net
worth, including the increase due to inflation, deriving from net
income from operations. These profits are fully reflected in net
worth (Annex 2.4).
(b) Official Funds. The Government's direct contribution, as defined
in Resolution 37 of the Monetary Board is paid fully to FEN in
1984 (Annex 2.5).
As prescribed by CONPES, 4% of increase in legal reserves related
to CDTs placed by financial institutions is received by FEN, as of
January 1, 1984. (The figures for 1984 are FEN estimates.) From
1985 to 1987, the CDTs will increase at a rate which includes the
combined effects of the rate of inflation, the real growth rate of
the economy, an increment in monetization,1/ and a decrease in the
participation of the CDTs in total transferrable financial
savings.2/ The resulting rate is 25.10% for 1985; 25.22% for 1986
and 25.69% for 1987.
The 30% of IFI's share of ISS's constant value bonds is received by
FEN in 1984. Through 1987, ISS's constant value bonds are expected
to increase at a rate which includes the combined effects of the
rate of inflation,3/ and the growth rate of the formal labor
force4/ (2% annually). The resulting rate is 26.48% for 1984;
22.4% for 1985, 1986 and 1987.
The results are shown in Annex 2.5.
1/   The ratio of transferable financial savings to gross domestic product
grew from 13% in 1976 to 22% in 1981 and 1982. It was projected as
follows: 22.0% in 1983; 22.7% in 1984; 23.5% in 1985; 24.2% in 1986 and
25.0% in 1987.
2/   1.5% annually.
3/   A proxy for the rate of interest in formal nominal wages.
4/   The growth of the formal labor force is equal to total labor growth
minus informal labor.



- 64 -
Annex 2.2
Page 2 of 2
(c) Local Investors. The amount of funds that FEN can obtain in the
domestic capital market were calculated in Chapter 3.
Assets and Uses
2.        Projected assets and uses are based on the following assumptions:
(a) Liquidity. FEN's liquidity is projected based upon the assumption
that FEN would maintain a liquidity buffer comprised of the equiva-
lent of two months of estimated annual expenses, as reflected in
FEN's income statement, plus one month of the estimated principal
amount that would become due and payable to local investors during
the year in question. This would serve to cushion FEN against
asynchronic collections and payments, and/or to provide secondary
liquidity (Annex 2.13).
(b) Credit to Power Companies. The total amount of outstanding credit
to the power companies at the end of each year is the difference
between total assets and liabilities, and liquidity and fixed and
other assets, at the same date. In turn, this credit is divided
into outstanding FDE credits and others. New (net) credit to the
power companies during each year is the difference between that
outstanding at the end and at the beginning of the year (Annex
2.13).
(c) Fixed and Other (Net) Assets. These amount to 1% of the FEN's net
worth (Annex 2.13).
A Summary of the Projections
3.        Annex 2.13 summarizes FEN's assets and liabilities, and sources and
uses of funds during 1983-87.

i/  By Monetary Board Resolution No. 37 of 1982, BR is authorized to invest
Col$3,000 million in FEN's CDTs, or other paper that FEN may issue, pro-
vided that these proceeds are used exclusively to finance medium and
long-term loans to the electricity companies. BR's investment is to be
effected by using 4% of the incremental legal reserves held by BR deriving
from the CDTs placed by financial institutions. FEN will pay interest of
0.5% above the rate that BR pays the financial institutions for the funds
(currently about 21%). BR would renew its investment in FEN semi-annual-
ly, but there is no assurance that these funds would be reinvested in-
definitely. Additionally, CONPES has recommended assigning to FEN 4%
annually of the increase in the reserves deriving from these CDTs, begin-
ning January 1, 1983. For the purposes of these financial projections,
however, it is assumed that the funds would be invested in FEN beginning
in 1984.

increased from Col$1,113 million in 1979 to Col$2,328 million in 1982, as
verified by the Controlaria General de la Republica. The funds are repay-
able by FEN over 25 years at an interest rate of 5.5% over inflation.
