Annex 3.6
Page 1 of 6
PROJECTIONS OF THE HOLDINGS OF FINANCIAL ASSETS
1.        This annex describes the methods used to project the holdings of
financial assets in Colombia during the period 1982-87. Shortly, the
procedure consisted of: (a) estimating demand functions for financial
assets; (b) deriving income elasticity coefficients from (a) above; (c)
projecting holdings of financial assets on the basis of income elasticity
coefficients and alternative assumptions on the performance of the Colombian
economy to 1987.
Demand Functions
2.        Demand functions for real financial assets were estimated on the
basis of quarterly data supplied by the Banco de la Republica for the period
1974-81. Given the deep changes that occurred in the Colombian financial
sector as of 1974, no attempt was made to extend the sample period to earlier
years. The use of quarterly data, thus, yielded 32 observations.
3.        Quarterly estimates of nominal GDP are the preliminary result of
work done by Dr. Martha Lee, of the Research Department of the Banco de la
Republica. They are not official estimates yet. These data, as well as the
data on holdings of financial assets, were deflated with the wholesale price
index. The data used in the regression analysis appears in Table A.1.



- 83 -
Annex 3.6
Page 2 of 6
4.        Regression equations were estimated through ordinary least squares,
according to the following formats:
(1)  LM1K    a + bLGDPK + cLTEI + dlDl + d2D2 + d3D3
(2) LRAMK = a + bLGDPK + cLTEI + dlDl + d2D2 + d3D3
where
LM1K =        Log of currency and demand deposits (Ml),
in real terms.
LGDP =        Log of GDP, in real terms.
LTEI =        Log of average interest rate on interest earning,
financial assets.
D1, D2, D3 = Seasonal (quarterly) dummy variables (1,0).
LRAMK =       Log of other financial assets, in real terms.
5.        The detailed results of the regression analysis appear in Tables
A.2 and A.3. The estimated equations explain 91% of the variation in the



- 84 -
Annex 3.6
Page 3 of 6
dependant variable in the case of equation (1) and 93% of the variation in
the dependant variable in the case of equation (2). The income elasticity
coefficients are 1.26 for currency and demand deposits and 3.86 for other
financial assets; both are significantly different from zero at the 1% signi-
ficance level. The interest rate variable is significantly negative in
equation (1) and not significant in equation (2).
6.        The income elasticity coefficient for Mi, 1.26, is quite consistent
with other estimates of the demand for money in Colombia and can be used
without further ado for projection purposes. However, the income elasticity
coefficient for other financial assets is extremely high, 3.86, reflecting
the rapid growth of the financial sector after the liberalizing reforms of
1974. It would be misleading to use this coefficient for the projection
period because the effects of the liberalizing reforms can now be considered
exhausted, and no further liberalization is expected.
7.        In order to choose an elasticity coefficient for projecting  other
financial assets, an analysis was done of the sensitivity of this variable
(coefficient b in equation (2) above) with respect to the choice of sample
period for regression analysis. The procedure adopted consisted of
estimating equation (2) for successively shorter sample periods (i.e.
starting with 1974-1981, continuing with 1975-1981, 1976-1981, etc.) The
full results are presented in Table A.4. The elasticity coefficients
estimated for different sample periods were:



- 85 -
Annex 3.6
Page 4 of 6
Sample   Elasticity    Standard    Adjusted    Number of
Period   Coefficient   Deviation  R_             Observations
1974-81       3.86          .43         .92            32
1975-81       4.13          .34         .93            28
1976-81      3.72           .52         .88            24
1977-81       1.37          .55         .87            20
1978-81       1.37          .94         .78            16
1979-81       1.88         1.15         .92            12
1980-81       2.11         1.41         .91             8
8.        The estimated elasticity coefficient declines sharply when the
years 1974, 1975, and 1976 are eliminated from the sample period.  The
minimum of the estimated coefficients, 1.37, was the one chosen for
projecting "other financial assets."
9.        On the basis of these income elasticities, and of the macroeconomic
hypothesis outlined in the main text, holdings of financial assets were
projected as follows.

Attachment 1
Page 1 of 3
Mesitas Hydroelectric Project - Loan 1628-CO
Supplemental Financing
Background
1.        The Mesitas Hydroelectric Power Project, consisting of:  Pumping
Station for Muna Head Pond, El Paraiso Power Plant (270 MW), La Guaca Power
Plant (330 MW), the necessary transmission works, and the strengthening of
the Sesquile dam was appraised by the Bank in late 1977 - early 1978. A loan
of US$84 million to EEEB (para 1.14) was approved by the Board in November
1978 and the loan becam- effective in August 1979. The project, designed to
generate about 2000 Gwh/year in an average year, was originally scheduled to
be completed in early 1982. Due to a number of reasons, explained below,
completion of the generation and transmission components is now expected in
mid-1984. The strengthening of the Sesquile dam will be completed in 1985.
Original cost estimate and financing plan
2.        The total estimated cost of the project (excluding interest during
construction) (See Attachment 1) at the time of appraisal was US$260.8
million of which US$148.4 million (57%) was foreign. The financing plan that
was envisaged at that time considered US$54.2 from suppliers credits and
commercial banks, US$10.2 from FED (Fondo de Desarrollo Electrica) and EEEB
would finance the local cost amounting to US$112.4 million from its own
internal cash generation.
Project Implementation
3.        Project execution encountered one serious geological problem in
executing the underground works. During the Granada II tunnel excavation a
soft rock spot was hit and had to be bypassed. This caused a delay of more
than 12 months. Additional delays were caused by financial difficulties that
EEEB faced in 1981 and 1982 mainly due to a loss of revenues of about US$40
million in 1981 due to rationing imposed by severe hydrological conditions -
a very dry year. These financial difficulties in turn contributed to the
bankruptcy of the contractor responsible for the construction of the two
power houses. In 1983 the situation improved because EEEB was able to
contract the construction of the power houses with another contractor and no
more difficulties are now expected
Revised cost estimate
4.        As can be seen from the attached cost estimates, the higher cost of
US$145.5 million is due to:
a) increase in the base cost of the civil works due to (1)
increased cost of tunnelling because of geological difficulties
and (2) the need to build an additional spillway to assure the
security of the Mesitas complex.



- 92 -
ANNEX 4.1
Attachment 1
Page 2 of 3
b) increase in the base cost of equipment, in particular the
penstock.
c) price contingencies are more than double the original estimate
due to a major part to higher inflation in the earlier years
than expected and to a minor part to the two years delay in
project completion.
5.        Notwithstanding the substantial cost increase, the project, the
scope of which could not be decreased since the components in the sizes
originally planned have been committed, remains economically justified.
a) The cost per Kw installed (calculated in >98O prices) of Mesitas
is US$670, which compares favorably with the unit cost for
Guavio of US$800 and for Guadalupe of US$670, two other
hydroelectric projects which the Bank is financing partially in
Colombia.
b) The internal rate of return of the project has increased
with respect to the appraisal estimate as electricity tariffs
(the measure of benefits) have increased 66% more than cost
(1983 figures).
6.        The new financing plan is shown with the attached cost estimates.
It envisages that FEN would lend a total of US$62.9 million to cover a part
of the foreign cost overrun of Mesitas. US$43.2 million would be financed by
Bank funds and US$19.7 would be financed by funds from the commercial banks.
All disbursements would be made against contracts let under the Bank's
guidelines. The local cost overrun would be financed by EEEB from its own
internal cash generation. The US$43.2 million includes US$12 million of
retroactive financing. I
February 24, 1984

- 133 -
Annex 5.40
COLOMBIA
FEN POWER DEVELOPMENT FINANCE
Financial Projection Parameters
1.          The electric sector financial consolidation consists of an aggre-
gation of the financial statements of the individual electric companies,
forecast on a homogeneous basis. The inter-company accounts and transactions
that cancel out each other have been compensated for or eliminated in two
steps: (a) ICEL and CORELCA and their "electrificadoras"; and (b) ISA and
its shareholders. Several simplifications to correct for different account-
ing or projecting approaches have been used in calculating certain global
items (i.e. purchased energy, other operating costs, ncn-operating income,
etc.); therefore, some consolidated financial items may differ slightly from
the individual original statements. These adjustments give the consolidation
an acceptable degree of uniformity and coherence. However, minor errors may
subsist.
2.          Basic projection parameters were agreed with ISA and its share-
holders in early 1982: (a) energy balance: based on an agreed projected
demand, sector expansion and generation programs, energy inter-changes,
tariff for interchanges in the interconnected system, commissioning sche-
dules, construction programs (updated in May 1983 and February 1984); (b)
estimated international and domestic inflation rates and rates of exchange as
projected at that time (Table 1 below); (c) annual asset revaluation using
estimated domestic inflation rates; (d) tariff increases projected indivi-
dually by each company, according to its particular financial situation; (e)
depreciations, operating costs and working capital requirements estimated
with adequate projection criteria based on the recent historic experience;
(f) capital contributions from national budgetary resources to ICEL, CORELCA
and ISA (through CORELCA for the 500-kV Interconnection); (g) reasonable
availability of external financing from international institutions, commer-
cial banks -- through FEN or directly -- and suppliers for foreign components
of construction programs; and (h) availability of local financing from: (i)
(refinancing of long-term debt and local component of construction programs);
and (ii) FONADE, for studies.
Table 1: EXPECTED INFLATION RATES AND RATES OF EXCHANGE 1/
Average            Annual
International    Local    Rate of Exchange    Devaluation 2/
%            %           $Col/$US                %
1982            8.5         25.0             63.90              16.5
1983            7.5          23.0            73.50              25.0
1984            7.5         22.0             84.10              14.5
1985            7.5          20.0            95.10              13.0
1986            6.0          18.0           106.50              12.0
1987            6.0          18.0           119.30              12.0
1/ Expected inflation rates reflect annual calendar rates.
2/ Rate between annual averages.
